MASTER THESIS
==PAGE:2==
Page 2 of 119
This report documents our master thesis within Design & Innovation 
engineering at the Technical University of Denmark. The report is a 
result of a ﬁve-month long thesis project completed in the spring of 
2020, corresponding to 30 ECTS points per student. The thesis is a 
collaboration between the master students Anna Villebro Rasmussen 
and Kathrine Jerichau Nissen, Herlev Hospital and Professor Michael 
Deininger. 
A special thanks to Hanne Konradsen, research nurse at Herlev Hos-
pital, for reaching out and initiating this project, and to Michael Dei-
ninger for excellent supervision throughout the project.
Furthermore, we want to thank all the nurses, doctors and other 
healthcare professionals that have helped us during the process. 
Especially: Henriette Thomsen, Soﬁe Philipsen, Cecilie Max Anders, 
Sanne Hosholm Dragsted and Marie Lommer Bagger.
In addition, we want to thank Mette Villebro and Klaus Sievers for a 
thorough review. 
ACKNOWLEDGEMENT
Anna Villebro Rasmussen, s143955   
Kathrine Jerichau Nissen, s143972
==PAGE:3==
Page 3 of 119
The purpose for this report is to document the process, development 
and solution for the master thesis project: Concept Development on 
Monitoring Fluid Intake for Patients. 
The goal for this project is to develop a solution that can mitigate or 
improve upon some of the problems surrounding ﬂuid balance mon-
itoring. A vital part of nursing care is to monitor the patient’s ﬂuid 
balance. Tracking the oral intake of ﬂuids is currently done with a 
handheld ﬂuid balance chart. This procedure poses several issues like 
forgetting to register, double documentation and illegible handwrit-
ing, which then results in untrustworthy data and high likelihood of 
malpractice.
The process of the project was designed by combining different de-
sign approaches and outlined as a new Combined Design-Approach 
model. Using Agile thinking permitted the project to go through sev-
eral iterations enabling the ideas to evolve into a ﬁnal solution with 
close user engagement. The different design tools and methods were 
applied appropriately through the process to either diverge or con-
verge the problem and solution space. The process ended up having 
six iterations besides the initial problem scoping, going from 14 ideas 
to a ﬁnal solution: EquTjek. 
EquTjek introduces a more streamlined documentation process 
through an app that is integrated in the nurses existing rovers and 
the nurses’ work ﬂow in general. This results in; efﬁcient & user-friend-
ly documentation, less likelihood of malpractice, trustworthy data and 
enabling patient involvement.
The complexity within the healthcare system is very high and the 
tacit knowledge existing in nursing practice can be difﬁcult to grasp. 
Having a close collaboration with the Hospital and other relevant ac-
tors in the healthcare system, has proven to be especially beneﬁcial 
for this kind of project. These learnings have been formulated into 
recommendations to communicate the most important ﬁndings.

Introduction
The future of healthcare is facing great challenges. The ﬁve meg-
atrends identify that the healthcare system needs to be improved if 
we are to uphold the quality and increasing demand. Within a couple 
of years, the demography will chance in Denmark; there will be more 
elderly who needs treatment and fewer able-bodied workers to help. 
The expectations for treatment will simultaneously increase together 
with the amounts of diseases that can be cured. To accommodate 
these challenges, there is a need for more patient involvement and 
digitalisation of nursing tasks. 
One of the areas that can be improved upon and streamlined is the 
ﬂuid balance monitoring. Fluid balance is extremely important to 
monitor for some patients, since it can lead to prolonged hospital 
stays, re-hospitalisations, heart failure, brain damage or in worst case 
death. However, the ﬂuid balance is currently recorded using an inef-
ﬁcient and problematic handheld ﬂuid balance chart, which is often 
neglected, and the data is mistrusted. In fact, a study found that 75% 
of all ﬂuid balance charts are ﬁlled out incorrectly (Jeyapala, 2015). This 
means that 75% of the patients on ﬂuid balance are at risk of malprac-
tice. Furthermore, prolonged hospital stays and rehospitalizations are 
a huge expenditure for the Danish healthcare system.
The goal for this report is to develop a solution that can mitigate or 
eliminate the problems surrounding the ﬂuid balance charts and 
thereby increase patient safety, increase nurses motivation for record-
ing the oral ﬂuid intake, and provide better care, and as a result devel-
op a solution that will accommodate the ﬁve megatrends. 
The Project Setup
The project was initiated at Herlev Hospital by research nurse Hanne 
Konradsen who reached out to Michael Deininger, Professor at DTU. 
Together they formulated a project description upon the need for a 
better process for registering ﬂuid intake at the wards. The project 
sparked our interest and motivation and we applied for it. Figure 1 
illustrated the three stakeholder groups in the project. 
Figure 1 - The collaboration setup
The project has been conducted in close collaboration with Herlev 
Hospital. The Gastroenterology ward has been the starting point, but 
other wards have been involved as well. Hanne Konradsen has been 
arranging visits, meetings and interviews with relevant personnel at 
Herlev Hospital throughout the project. We have been initiating the 
need for meetings and visits and Hanne has provided us with dates 
and stakeholders.

Motivation 
Our motivation for this project has several perspectives. Firstly, we are 
very engaged and interested in the ﬁeld of medical devices and solu-
tions within the healthcare system. Secondly, we are motivated by the 
project setup with Herlev Hospital, making it possible to engage mul-
tiple healthcare professionals in the development process. Thirdly the 
project has a potential for evolving into a start-up and in the future 
becoming a viable business. 
The Field of Healthtech 
We are highly motivated by the interesting and complex problem 
statement which entails many possibilities for producing innovative 
and exciting solutions. The myriad possibilities of the problem state-
ment give us an eagerness to explore and understand the problem 
and to make sure that it will be solved in the best way possible. Anoth-
er motivator is being able to make a difference and to know that our 
solution might be able to reduce some pressure on the very busy and 
hardworking healthcare professionals at Herlev Hospital. 
Co-Creation with Herlev Hospital
The project posed an opportunity to co-create a solution in close col-
laboration with Herlev Hospital. It is a huge motivator to be able to 
quickly get in contact with users and thereby create solutions that 
will work appropriately. Furthermore, the need originated at Herlev 
Hospital which entails that we can help solve a real-world problem. 
Start-up Possibility
We have an interest in making a solution that can become a viable 
business after the end of the project. This is possible due to the ﬁeld, 
the problem and the setup. The solution will be able to be upscaled to 
other hospitals and other countries. 
Learning Objectives
Several learning objectives were constructed as a part of the project.  
A student who has met the objectives of the course will be able to:
Plan, structure and execute relevant design methods that 
support the design process to go from a basic need to a solu-
tion in a complex system. 
Identify and deﬁne complex needs within the healthcare 
sector in order to design products, services and systems that 
successfully address these.
Evaluate and reframe design challenges and problems to 
navigate the needs and interests of various stakeholders 
from different backgrounds.
Manage and stage relevant ﬁeld work, collect data, and use 
it actively and prioritized in the development of solutions. 
Apply user experience and behavioural design theories and 
methods in the development of the solutions. 
Involve, engage and collaborate with multiple relevant stake-
holders in the appropriate stages of the design process.
Apply rapid prototyping skills to improve staging of us-
er-tests.
Apply theories of innovation, business strategy and entre-
preneurship to the project.

Goals for the Project
The main goal for the project is to develop a solution that can resolve 
issues and improve the procedure of monitoring ﬂuid balance while 
achieving the learning objectives. The project needs to go from prob-
lem scoping to development of a ﬁnal solution. To ensure that the 
solution will ﬁt into the nurses’ workﬂow, a close collaboration with 
the stakeholders will be a central element in the process. 
We aim for developing a solution that will:
 •
Holistically ﬁt into the nurses’ current workﬂow and their needs 
 •
Increase the patient care and safety 
 •
Mitigate malpractice    
 •
Have a potential for creating a start-up 
Reading Guide
Report Structure
The report is structured with six chapters describing the essential 
parts of the project. The six chapters are: 
Intro
The intro provides several perspectives on the project goals, motiva-
tion, objectives etc.
Background and Problem
This chapter described the essential knowledge obtained throughout 
the project and is needed to understand the whole problem and the 
context surrounding it. 
Framework & Methods
This chapter explains how we have scoped the problem. It introduces 
several approaches for the design process and provides an overview 
of the methods and tools used in the project. 
From Problem to Solution
This chapter gives an overview of the actual process. The chapter out-
lines the activities conducted during the process. 
The Final Solution - EquTjek 
Here the ﬁnal solution is presented. The result of our project. 
Outro
In the outro the project is evaluated and discussed, recommenda-
tions are provided and further work is described. 
In each chapter an introduction to the content within it is provided 
along with highlights in the end of each chapter.

daily nursing practice in general and what problems they face will 
be uncovered. Furthermore, the Danish healthcare system and the 
ﬁve megatrends within healthcare will be analysed on a holistic level 
to fully provide an insight to the whole system.

The Importance of Fluid Balance
A part of the process of treating a patient sometimes requires FB 
monitoring. In fact, around 15% of all patients in Denmark require FB 
monitoring (MeasureLet, 2020). During the daily ward rounds, the 
doctor prescribes FB monitoring for the patient if needed. This means 
that the nurse needs to record oral ﬂuid intake, amongst other things.
Fluid balance refers to the ﬂuid balance in our bodies. Females con-
tain 52% ﬂuids, and males contain 60%. When we are healthy our ﬂu-
id balance will be around those percentages, ﬂuctuating within 1% 
(Shepherd, 2011). When people are hospitalised and suffering from 
certain diseases, their ﬂuid balance needs to be monitored. A ﬂuid im-
balance can happen when a patient loses more ﬂuid than their body 
can take in or if the patient takes in more ﬂuid than their body can get 
rid of (Martin, 2017).
A poorly completed ﬂuid balance registration can result in persisting 
overhydration or dehydration, which can thereby result in (Thomp-
son, 2016):
 •
Delayed and/or incorrect medical review
 •
Unexpected deterioration in patients
 •
Inappropriate medication dosages or medical malpractice
 •
Prolonged symptoms
 •
Prolonged hospital stays
 •
Brain damage
 •
Death
A list of the most common diseases that need ﬂuid balance monitor-
ing are:
 •
Renal/kidney diseases
 •
Cardiovascular diseases (heart diseases)
 •
Diabetes
 •
Hyponatremia/hypernatremia (irregular salt balance)
 •
Gastroenterological diseases (stomach/bowel diseases)
 •
Geriatric diseases (elderly diseases)
These are some of the frequently occurring diseases, but there are 
many other situations where FB monitoring is needed, e.g. patients 
with acute illnesses in the Intensive Care Unit, viruses etc. (NHS trust, 
2020)
Fluid balance is monitored by registering the inputs and outputs of 
the patient, see ﬁgure 2. Keeping track of these will lead to an under-
standing of whether the patient is consuming ﬂuids according to the 
treatment plan.
Figure 2 - The inputs and outputs of ﬂuid balance
==PAGE:15==
Page 15 of 119
The nurses operate with two kinds of ﬂuid balance registrations: Ex-
tended ﬂuid balance and regular ﬂuid balance. In the extended ver-
sion all seven factors: Probe, medicine, IV ﬂuids, oral intake, puke, 
urine & faeces and perspiration & evaporation, need to be registered. 
In other cases, it can be enough to track only the input: meaning 
probe, medicine, IV ﬂuids and the oral intake, see ﬁgure 3. Due to the 
high level of documentation that the nurses are obligated to perform; 
probe, Medicine and IV ﬂuid should be registered even if the patient 
is not on ﬂuid balance registration (Field work, 27th of January 2020).
The diseases can also be divided into ﬂuid prescription and ﬂuid re-
striction. Patients that are on ﬂuid prescription need to drink more 
than they are used to and often forget to drink if the nurses do not re-
mind them. Patients on ﬂuid restriction are often suffering from heart 
or kidney failure, and as a side effect from their medication, they get 
very thirsty. It is vital that they do not drink more than up to a certain 
limit or it can have serious consequences.
Measuring Fluid Balance
To keep track of the ﬂuid balance a ﬂuid balance chart is used. The 
chart is a piece of A4 paper with a table on it. The hospitals and the 
different wards have different charts depending on the area of spe-
cialisation for the ward. Some wards focus on nutrition which is then 
included in the chart as well. The procedure on how to register ﬂuid 
is the same. See two examples of ﬂuid balance charts illustrated as an 
artifact model in ﬁgure 4.
The nurse registers both what is offered to the patient and what is 
consumed during the day. Often the patients get a whole jug of e.g. 
water, this allows the patient to pour what he wants to drink and then 
the nurse can see how much the ﬂuid has decreased in the jug. Even 
though the ﬂuid balance chart seems very easy and straightforward 
to use, it generates a lot of issues for the nurses.
Nursing Practice 
To work as a nurse is to care holistically for the patients, meaning they 
do not prescribe any treatment, but takes care of the patients’ basic 
needs like showering, getting something to eat, while also providing 
the medicine and treating acute illnesses like cardiac arrest and bed-
sores. In other words, the nurses core task is to help the patient recov-
er from their diseases as best and quickly as possible (Salling Larsen, 
2012). The tasks can vary a lot depending on where the nurse works 
e.g. working in home care requires different priorities than working in 
a hospital. Even the different wards in the same hospital have varying 
workﬂows. A nurse in a Danish hospital cares for appr. 6-8 patients at

==PAGE:17==
Page 17 of 119
a time. This can vary though, from 1-2 nurses per patient in the inten-
sive care unit, to 1 nurse having 15-20 patients during a night shift with 
help from social and health care assistants (Tacit Knowledge Hotline, 
2020).
 
A common part of the daily routines for nurses in hospital wards are 
the daily meetings and updates with the rest of the staff. The meet-
ings provide time for the nurses to hand-over the patients to the nurse 
running the next shift. Information like the status of the condition for 
the patient in general, and reminders on speciﬁc needs, like FB mon-
itoring can be transferred.
 
The treatment plan for the patient is made during the daily ward 
rounds, where both nurse and doctor is present. This lays the founda-
tion for what the nurse must do besides basic care. The doctor pre-
scribes and the nurse executes.
Besides caring for the patients, the nurses also must document every 
move and activity happening during their shift. This means that they 
end up spending appr. 50% of their shift documenting their care in SP 
(Field work, 27th of January 2020). Time that would be much better 
spent caring for the patient. A simpliﬁed overview of the daily FB tasks 
for a nurse is illustrated as a sequence model in worksheet 1. As shown 
in the worksheet, the nurse has around 5,5 to 7,5 minutes pr. activity 
that needs to be documented. These minutes include basic care, doc-
umentation of the activity in SP and the actual activity happening, 
e.g. giving medicine. The model is a highly simpliﬁed overview, but it 
clariﬁes why many nurses complain about time insufﬁciency and low 
quality of patient care.
 
In 2015 Dansk Sygeplejeråd made a campaign called Time for Quality 
(red. Danish: Tid til kvalitet). This campaign wanted to put focus on the 
nurses running too fast, due to too many tasks caused by a growing 
amount of patients and a decrease in the number of nurses (Dansk 
Sygeplejeråd, 2015).
There have been several efforts to streamline the documentation pro-
cess. The nurses have, besides the computers in the nurse ofﬁce, a 
rover, which is a form of handheld smartphone with a built-in scanner, 
and a computer station on a roller table. A description of the technol-
ogy is illustrated as an artifact model in worksheet 2. The smartphone 
resembling Zebra rover is meant for the nurse to be able to be with 
the patient while documenting, however SP is not very user-friendly 
in general and especially not on smartphones.
The nurses receive education on how to use the new technological 
systems and artifacts. This is done by having one nurse from every 
ward attend education, who then becomes responsible for teaching 
the rest of the nurses in their respective wards. This results in some 
wards being efﬁcient and use all the newest technology, and other 
wards lagging behind, since the educated nurses do not necessarily 
have any technological ingenuity. 
 
Documentation of patient information and treatment in a patient 
journal has been required by law in Denmark since 2013. This is due to 
patient safety and for use in case of complaints (Dansk Sygeplejeråd, 
2020).
 
Although SP has all the necessary information, the nurses typically 
print out an A4 sheet with the patient’s information, to get an easy 
overview of their tasks and to help remembering different important 
details.
Electronic Patient Journals 
The ‘Sundhedsplatform’ is a response to the ﬁve megatrends in 
healthcare, that demands that hospitals, regions and general prac-
titioners work digitally and closer together amongst other initiatives 
(the ﬁve megatrends are described under the section: ‘A holistic over-
view’). The SP is an important step towards securing that hospitals 
can accommodate the future high demand of digitalisation, new 
technologies and efﬁcient use of data.
 
==PAGE:18==
Page 18 of 119
It consists of one joint system for the healthcare staff, replacing 30 old 
systems. For all hospitals to work within the same digital system, re-
ceiving constant patient data updates and quicker response time on 
e.g. blood tests, scanning etc. The patients can also get an overview 
of their own treatment across different departments, hospitals and 
regions using the online platform: ‘Min sundhedsplatform’. (Regionh.
dk, 2020)
 
SP is produced by the American company Epic and has been imple-
mented in all hospitals in the Capital Region of Denmark and Region 
Zealand in 2017. (Regionh.dk, 2020).
 
However, the SP has received a lot of negative criticism since its launch 
in 2016. A satisfaction survey on the SP from 2019 ended with 67% of 
the responding doctors being either ‘dissatisﬁed’ or ‘very dissatisﬁed’ 
(Regionh.dk, 2019). This translates into two out of three doctors being 
dissatisﬁed. Chief physician Peter Marckmann at Roskilde hospital 
labelled this a crisis. He believes that the risks of malpractice have 
increased since its implementation, simply because it works so badly 
(Juul and Würtz, 2020). The nurses however are a bit more satisﬁed: 
37% of the responding nurses were either ‘dissatisﬁed’ or ‘very dissat-
isﬁed’, a 30%-points better score than for the doctors, see ﬁgure 5.
The other three regions in Denmark have chosen another EPJ sys-
tems (EPJ = Electric patient journal) provided by Systematics. This 
system has, contrary to SP, received mostly positive critique, clearly 
demonstrated in a survey where younger doctors rated the two EPJ 
system using the Danish ‘7-trin skala’ grading system: (Drachmann, 
2020), see ﬁgure 6. 
The SP fails by receiving a grade below 02, and the Systematics MidtEPJ 
receives a good grade of 9,2 which is considered above average.
Figure 5 - SP Satisfaction survey
Figure 6 - Grading the EPJs, from survey

The Problems Surrounding FBCs 
What is the Problem?
The FBCs are notorious for creating problems. All articles found in the 
literature review claims that major parts of the FBCs are not ﬁlled in 
correctly, see ﬁgure 7. 
Figure 7 - Literature review on compliance for FBC.
This suggests that the current method for registering OFB is very in-
efﬁcient and in general just not working well. There are numerous 
reasons for this:
 •
Incorrect and hasty notations on the FBC leads to misunderstand-
ings during shift handovers and also results in incorrect data due 
to the illegible handwriting.
 •
The FBC method requires so-called double documentation; ﬁrst 
the nurse registers the ﬂuid on the handheld A4 paper, then does 
a calculation of all numbers noted on the chart, and ﬁnally the 
complete registration can enter SP. 
 •
SP is not user-friendly. The nurse can enter the notations in myriad 
different ways. This means that the registration can be recorded 
differently by all nurses. Confusion arises concerning where to look 
and register in SP. This problem might not be the case in the re-
gions using Systematics EPJ.
 •
The nurses do not trust the data in SP since the data is often not 
recorded due to the non-user-friendly system. This problem might 
not be the case in the regions using Systematics EPJ.
 •
The untrustworthy data is also due to nurses often forgetting to 
register the OFB data.
 •
The overloaded nurses will currently always de-prioritise FB tasks 
compared to other tasks. FB tasks does not excite or energize the 
nurses like giving medicine or like treating acute cardiac arrest will 
do.
These problems are also supported by the answers from our survey 
made to evaluate ideas and create a baseline. The respondents were

asked to evaluate the current FBC method and estimate how much

49,6% of the respondents evaluated the FBC as ‘Bad’ or ‘Very bad’, and 
20,3% evaluated the solution as ‘Good’ or ‘Very good’. This indicates 
that the FBC is in general dis-satisfactory. The respondents spend a 
mean of 15 minutes registering FB. 
The problems were observed during our ﬁeld work from an engineer-
ing perspective. The literature in this area also lists several reasons, 
see worksheet 3 for literature review. 
 •
A lack of awareness about the importance of FB
 •
Inadequate training of staff
 •
Lack of general awareness upon ﬂuid registration
 •
Poorly designed FBC’s 
 •
Time management & lack of time
Most articles surrounding this problem recommend a general solu-
tion by increasing FB education and awareness for the nurse, while 
also suggesting vaguely described ideas for a more user-friendly FBC. 
These suggestions are mostly temporary solutions that would need 
to be repeated often.

How big is the Problem?
From research online it is clear that the methods for FB registrations 
are very similar to the Danish method in many European countries, 
and It is most likely a worldwide problem. It seems that many Euro-
pean countries including Great Britain are struggling with the same 
FB registration issues as in Denmark. In other words, the problems 
expand beyond the Danish borders and a solution could be beneﬁcial 
for many countries, especially ones with a public healthcare system. 
The many different origins of the articles in the literature review about 
FB problems is the reasoning behind this assumption.
A Holistic Perspective on Healthcare
The Danish healthcare system is provided by the Danish government 
as a part of the Danish welfare model and is a central part of the Dan-
ish welfare society. This stems from a desire to have easy and equal 
opportunities of treatment, meaning that receiving treatment should 
not be dependent upon income but rather the need for treatment 
(Whitta-Jacobsen, 2013). The hospitals primary goal is to get the pa-
tients cured as best and as quickly as possible.
 
A holistic investigation of the Danish welfare system is made to get a 
deeper understanding of the system that the solution needs to func-
tion within.
Unique Data
Besides a robust and long-lasting healthcare system, Denmark is 
quite unique in the way that patient data is handled. The joint med-
ical card (red. Danish: Fælles medicinkort) is a system that gathers 
all patient data across all hospitals, general practitioners, home care 
nurses, nursing homes and medical specialists treating patients in 
Denmark. This system was implemented in 2011 for the purpose of 
(Sørensen, 2020):
 •
Reducing the amount of malpractice due to missing information 
about the patient’s medications when handling the prescription 
or when the patient is treated by different doctors.
 •
Reducing the number of readmissions due to malpractice.
 •
Reducing time spent for clarifying the patient’s current medical 
information. 
Patient Treatment Course and Hospitalisation
There are no completely identical patient treatment courses, these 
vary with regards to the patient’s condition and diseases. Further-
more, the different hospitals within the regions in Denmark have var-
ying procedures. However, a highly generalised overview of a patient 
treatment course is seen in ﬁgure 10 and 11 (Field work, 27th January 
2020).
Such a linear procedure is not realistic but can create an idea of the 
system on a holistic level. During the treatment course the patient 
will receive plans for the next steps and what the chances of recovery 
are.
Danish Welfare in Numbers
The expenses for healthcare in Denmark are covered by the Danish 
government, which makes it free for the citizens (who contribute via 
taxes). In 2017 16,4% of all public expenditures went to the healthcare 
system, corresponding to 183 billion DKK. This means that for every 
1000 DKK a Dane pays in taxes, 164 DKK goes to the health care sys-
tem (Dam, 2019). 
 
To put things in perspective: There are 622.000 (Danmarks statistik, 
2018, Sygehusbenyttelse) patients in DK each year. This accumulates 
to 3,9 million bed days each year (Danmarks statistik, 2018, IND03).
The cheapest rate for one bed day is 4800 DKK. (Sundhedsdatasty-
relsen, 2019). So hosting 622.000 patients a year with only basic care 
costs appr. 3 billion DKK. Of these 622.000 patients are 15% or 94.000 
patients on FB monitoring, see ﬁgure 12.

Societal Tendencies
Denmark, and in fact most countries in the world, are facing major 
healthcare challenges going forward. As our living standards and the 
technological development steadily gets better and better, new de-
velopments and improvements will be necessary to implement into 
the healthcare system during the next decades.  Otherwise it will be 
impossible to keep up the high quality and meet the high demands 
for treatment. These challenges are known as the ﬁve megatrends, 
concerning: (Kjellberg, 2017)
 •
A continuously aging population
During the next 20 years, a massive shift in the demography in 
Denmark will happen. There will be more elderly who generally 
will demand higher healthcare costs per person. There will also be 
fewer people in the working age population who are able to at-
tend to the increased demand.
 •
Higher prevalence of chronic diseases
The prevalence of chronic diseases will rise the coming years, due 
to better living standards and better treatments for previously 
life-threatening illnesses. Appr. 1/3rd of the population has one or 
more chronic diseases.
 •
The information revolution
During the next 10 years, the increasing amount of data and digi-
talisation will transform the healthcare system. It will demand new 
work procedures and automation of several tasks. 
 •
The clinical-technological development
The healthcare system is undergoing a transition from the me-
chanical to the biological era. Gene technology and other molecu-
lar biology is expected to revolutionise the healthcare system with-
in the next 10-15 years. 
 •
The future citizen as new healthcare consumer 
The future healthcare consumer will have increased expecta-
tions to the healthcare system, partly in relation to expectations 
for treatment, and partly by being involved and informed. Citizens 
who are capable, must to a greater extent be able to take care of 
themselves.
To accommodate these challenges the ﬁve regions of Denmark are 
building new super hospitals and modernizing the rest. This is meant 
to strengthen the patient care, heighten the efﬁciency, and overall 
make the hospitals ready for the future. (Godtsygehusbyggeri, 2020). 
With the new super hospitals comes new possibilities for optimisation 
of working procedures and integration of new technology. However, 
there will simultaneously be fewer hospital beds pr. citizen, lower la-
bour power pr. patient, so as a result, many patients will undertake 
responsibility for treatment at home, or have a more central role in 
the treatment process at the hospitals. This is made possible partly by 
the development of new digital solutions for healthcare tasks.
If the development of these trends continues, the healthcare expendi-
tures will increase by 47% before 2060 (Kjellberg, 2017).
Fact box: Bed days
a bed day is the term for a patient taking up one hospital bed and 
receiving basic care for 24 hours.
==PAGE:24==
Page 24 of 119
Legislations in Healthcare
When developing products or services for medical use, there are 
some special extensive legislations from both the EU and the Danish 
government that needs to be obeyed. Two of the main legislation are 
regarding safety of use and hygiene.
Safety
A product or service used as medical equipment needs to be CE la-
belled. A CE labelled product shows that the product complies with 
the legislations of the EU and can then freely move around in the 
European market. A CE labelled product presupposes that the equip-
ment has a high security level, is qualiﬁed for its purpose and that 
requirements for material safety and product robustness/stability are 
met. (Lægemiddelstyrelsen, 2020)
 
The rules for what can be classiﬁed as medical equipment covers a 
wide variety of products. But common to all is that any instrument, 
device, equipment, software, material or other object that is used on 
people in preparation for diagnosis, prevention, monitoring, treat-
ment or relief of diseases is classiﬁed as a medical equipment. A good 
rule of thumb is that any product or service that has an action or deci-
sion supporting inﬂuence on the treatment of an individual patient is 
likely classiﬁed as a medical equipment (Lægemiddelstyrelsen, 2020).
Hygiene
The most important factors for improving and keeping up hygiene 
is making sure that patients cannot infect other patients or nurses. 
There are some rather resistant, dangerous and tough viruses at risk 
of being spread inside the hospitals. Thus, very strict rules for hygiene 
procedures are enforced.
There exist many areas of expertise within hygiene in regards to prod-
ucts and services, but on a general level all equipment can be catego-
rized as non-critical, semi-critical or critical. 
If the equipment is non-critical meaning there is no intravenous con-
tact with the skin, then regular cleaning will be sufﬁcient.
If the equipment is semi-critical or critical, meaning that the equip-
ment is in contact with the mucosa or intravenous procedures, then 
more thorough disinfection or sterilisation is needed. If the patient 
has a viral infection, then more measures should be taken to disinfect 
the non-critical equipment. (Statens Serum Institut, 2017)
The design of physical products must have almost no grooves, holes 
or ridges where any microorganism reservoir formation can emerge. 
It is important that the surface is easy to clean and smooth to the 
touch. If there are any tricky places, then the products could be po-
tential carriers of bacteria, viruses etc. (Interview, Sanne Hosholm 
Dragsted, 6th of May 2020)
Technical devices like computer mice, keyboards, smartphones and 
tablets etc. have speciﬁed rules as well, since they most likely cannot 
endure heated disinfection. Instead, daily cleaning with a disinfecting 
cloth is the preferred procedure. These devices serve as a frequent 
contact surface that can contribute to microorganisms spreading in-
directly, which is why it is important to plan who, when and what are 
cleaning the devices daily in the wards (Statens Serum Institut, 2017).
Development Arena
A holistic overview of the Danish healthcare system has been made 
to increase knowledge of the system that the solution must ﬁt within, 
this is illustrated as a development arena in ﬁgure 13.
The development arena illustrates how the actors and arenas con-
nect and interact with each other, our project being the starting point 
for the arena’s composition. Our project interacts with FB as a con-
cept, Herlev Hospital, DTU and the competition. The other arenas are 
mostly indirectly inﬂuencing our project e.g. through legislations and 
societal tendencies.

The Target Users
This project revolves around two primary users: the nurses and the 
patents.
A generalised assumption-based description of the target group is 
made to get an understanding of their point of view, interests etc. 
followed by ﬁctional examples using personas.
Nurses
Nurses tend to be very caring and humanitarian people. Devoting 
one’s life to helping others survive illnesses is a very noble purpose. 
It suggests that nurses tend to think about having a purpose that 
beneﬁts others. When asked which tasks they liked the most dur-
ing ﬁeldwork, all four nurses answered that caring for and talking to 
the patients were their favourite tasks, while some were also thriving 
on the thrill of being able to help acute situations like cardiac arrest. 
When asked what their least favourite tasks were, they all answered 
the documentation process. (Field work, 30th January 2020)
It seems that nurses tend to crave action, purpose and an active work-
day, and like sitting in front of a computer all day is not desired. While 
others need peace and quiet to do their work, nurses thrive on action 
and visible results. These descriptions are assumptions based on in-
terviews. (Field work 30th January 2020)
Examples of two ﬁctional nurses are made as personas, to concretise 
the description, see ﬁgure 14.
Patients
Patients as a target group is hard to characterise. Hospitalised pa-
tients are generally older, see ﬁgure 15 (Statistikbanken.dk. 2020), but 
nevertheless every citizen young or old is a potential patient. Diseases 
do not only strike people who are at risk, it can strike everyone. How-
ever, it is possible to attach rough and generalised characteristics and 
demographics to the patients from department to department, or in 
other words, to the patients divided into their respective diseases. For 
example: Most patients with diabetes type 2 are older people or/and 
people with unhealthy lifestyles. However, patients diagnosed with 
diabetes type 1 are mostly healthy and under the age of 20.
Figure 15 - Bed days vs age
Regarding FB monitoring, many of the patients on ﬂuid prescription 
are suffering from geriatric diseases (elderly diseases) or gastroenter-
ological diseases (stomach/bowel diseases). In these departments, 
the patients have a higher prevalence of being fuddled and confused. 
This means that they are incapable of taking care of themselves or 
undertaking responsibility for part of their treatment. In other de-
partments where the patients are mostly on ﬂuid restriction, like 
cardiovascular (heart) or kidney diseases, the patient’s prevalence of 
confusion is less than that of the geriatric. They are more likely to be 
qualiﬁed for undertaking parts of their treatment (Field work, 27th

January 2020), and if they can, they must, to accommodate the ﬁve

some patients might have a high technical ingenuity, while others 
might have a hard time using any technical applications. There is a 
tendency for elderly people to have a hard time grasping new tech-

very hard to generalise. It is challenging to develop solutions that can 
satisfy such a wide array of characteristics, adding even more layers of 
complexity to the problem.

FRAMEWORK 
& METHODS
This chapter describes our approach to the process from problem to 
solution. Merging several principles of Agile, Stage-Gate, Double Dia-
mond and user involvement enabled us to make our own approach 
for this project. The design methods and tool used in the project are 
described and illustrated in a toolbox.

Our Design Process 
Our upcoming titles as design engineers provides us with a broad 
knowledge about different design processes and how to use these. In 
our master thesis process we have merged several design approaches 
to ﬁt our process for this project, context and theme. Borrowing and 
merging different methods enables us to support the process that 
we wanted to go through. Especially Agile development, the Double 
Diamond and the Stage-Gate model have formed our process.
Initial Planning
As a starting point for the thesis a project plan was made. This plan 
should ensure that the project went all the way from exploring the 
problem to a tested solution. See ﬁgure 17. The plan is based on differ-
ent phases of a design project containing: Exploration of context and 
technology, idea generation, exploration of ideas, testing and rede-
sign, ﬁnal prototype, ﬁnal test and deliverables. The speciﬁc phases 
were selected based on a combination of the Stage-Gate model and 
our previous project experiences. See fact box: Stage-Gate .
At the end of each phase milestones were planned to be sure that 
we were on schedule and that we managed to cover all phases in the 
end. Breaking down the overall project goal in milestones helped us 
to view the project from a more realistic and attainable angle. Smaller 
achievements can have a motivating factor.
Agile-Stage-Gate Hybrid Model
Although the Stage-Gate model follows a classic linear product de-
velopment process, we knew from the beginning that an agile mind-
set would beneﬁt the result in the end, yet still having a need for the 
Stage-Gate model as inspiration for the planning of the overall pro-
ject phases. To accommodate this, we chose to follow both an iter-
ative/agile process and the Stage-Gate process (Cooper, 2016). This 
process fusion is also known as the Agile-Stage-Gate Hybrid Model, 
which combines the use of an agile mindset that enabled us to iterate 
throughout our process, while also adhering to ﬁxed milestones.

Fact box: Stage-Gate
The Stage-Gate model is used as the development process 
by many companies all over the world (Stage-Gate Interna-
tional, 2020). The Stage-Gate takes its starting point from idea 
and follows the process all the way to launch of a product. The 
Stage-Gate model contains the following stages: Discover, 
scoping, build business case, development, testing and vali-
dation and launch and implementation, see ﬁgure 18. The dif-
ferent stages are well deﬁned and have deﬁned deliverables, 
which provides the project team with a clear goal before mov-
ing to the next phase. Furthermore, the gates make sure that 
decisions are made before moving on (Cooper, 2016).
Figure 18 - The Stage-Gate model
The iterative approach was selected due to the very complex problem 
and context that we were tapping into. Getting sufﬁcient knowledge 
about the problem and context would be almost impossible to obtain 
from the beginning. The mindset of agile enabled us to move forward 
in the process even though we did not possess a complete under-
standing of the problem, giving the right solution a breeding ground, 
and not forcing any wrong decisions. While developing the solutions 
the problem and the context were further explored and more knowl-
edge were achieved throughout the whole project.
Double Diamond Thinking
Within each phase of the process, both diverging and converging ac-
tivities were used. Exploring the opportunities within the phase (di-
verging) and subsequently making decisions (converging), ensured 
the projects progress. By applying a double diamond mindset, all al-
ternatives are explored and yet the project moves forward due to the 
continuous converging decisions.
Fact box:  Agile
Agile is a way of thinking. It was devised in the late 1990’s as 
an approach to developing software (Cooper, 2016). The Ag-
ile Manifesto binds together different methodologies around 
agile development as a set of guidelines. (Beck et al., 2001). In 
the agile manifesto the following lay the ground rules (Agile-
manifesto, 2012):
 •
Individuals and interactions over processes and tools
 •
Working software over comprehensive documentation
 •
Customer collaboration over contract negotiation
 •
Responding to change over following a plan
Agile thinking provides product developers and software 
developers with a more adaptive process serving the user’s 
needs ﬁrst.
Fact box:  The Agile–Stage‐Gate Hybrid Model
The hybrid model between the Stage-Gate and the Agile ap-
proach is becoming more and more popular for designing 
products. (Cooper, 2016)
Combining Stage-Gate and Agile is a newer approach for de-
veloping products, but several papers suggest the beneﬁts of 
combining the two. This makes the Stage-gate model more 
ﬂexible, allowing change and more interdisciplinary collabo-
ration in the stages of the product development (Ahmed-Kris-
tensen, 2015).
Gate 1 
Gate 2 
Gate 5 
Gate 4 
Gate 3 
==PAGE:34==
Page 34 of 119
er we would ensure a good ﬁt with their practices. Due to the origin of 
the project with Hanne Konradsen as a contact, we had good access 
to the end-users, especially the nurses.
Scrum Logbook and Worksheets
To accommodate our wish for an agile process we structured our daily 
work with the use of a Scrum logbook. This way of working is inspired 
by the principles of Scrum, but we only applied suitable parts of the 
Scrum framework. We held weekly sprints with a goal at the end 
of each week. The weekly goals would combined make us achieve 
the milestones. It enabled us to break down the work in managea-
ble pieces, which were written in the logbook. Planning the process 
continuously permitted changes to weekly goals according to how 
the process proceeded, which made us capable of shifting focus or 
changing direction (Rigby, 2016).
The logbook was used for documenting our tasks, and worksheets 
were used to document knowledge. The logbook was also used to 
write down reﬂections and decisions after the weeks, inspired by the 
sprint reviews, and served as a reminder of what, why and when we 
made any decisions or reﬂections.
Making worksheets was a good way to wrap up knowledge and 
present content. The worksheets have been used several times as a 
boundary object to mediate our ﬁndings or thoughts to the user or 
other stakeholders.
Fact box: Double Diamond 
The Double Diamond is a model that describes a design 
process from a problem to a solution. The model was made by 
the British Design Council in 2005. (Design Council, 2005) The 
model emphasises the divergent and convergent thinking 
and consists of the four phases: Discover, Deﬁne, Develop and 
Deliver, see ﬁgure 19. The divergent thinking is present in the 
discover and develop phase, where alternatives are explored. 
The convergent thinking is present in the deﬁne and deliver 
phases, where the many problems or ideas are narrowed 
down to a problem deﬁnition or a solution. (Lipiec, 2019)
Figure 19 - The Double Diamond Model
Including the End-User
Besides applying different approaches to our overall design process, 
we wanted to include the end-user as much as possible during the 
project. The valuable insights that we gain through interacting with 
the end-user are crucial to making a good design project. Especial-
ly within healthcare this approach has proven to be very important 
when designing new solutions (Center for Innovativ Medicinsk Te-
knologi, 2018). The participatory design brings together multiple re-
quirements from the different stakeholders, which minimizes the risk 
of having to rework the solution. With the engagement of the end-us-
Fact box: Worksheets
Worksheets are a tool for documenting data and thoughts. 
It is used for communication between actors, both internally 
and externally. It is good for organising the information with-
in a project and creating structure. (Hansen, 2006) The work-
sheets can contain everything from sketches, to calculations, 
reﬂections or decisions. The worksheet contains a header with 
information about the number, team name, subject, date etc.
==PAGE:35==
Page 35 of 119
Our Combined Process
Our process has been a combination of a wide variety of design pro-
cesses and tools. A model of the combination has been created and is 
illustrated in ﬁgure 21. 
This Combined Design-Approach Model has agile thinking, includes 
the users’ needs as essential to the whole process, has iterations that 
allow for sudden changes while also adhering to an overall goal divid-
ed into phases and a diverging and converging mindset that provides 
a framework for decision making. We believe that this model is the 
most suitable for complex product-system design processes.
Fact box: Scrum
Scrum is one of the most well-known agile frameworks. Scrum 
is originally used for software development but can be applied 
for a variety of different processes. Scrum is comprised of a 
scrum master, a scrum team, events, artifacts and rules, see 
ﬁgure 20. 
Figure 20 - The Scrum Framework
Scrum uses the term sprints as a time frame for iterations, 
which typically last for 2-4 weeks. One sprint contains one 
iteration and keeps repeating itself until the product backlog 
is complete. The product backlog contains the full set of the 
elements in the ﬁnal solution. Each sprint is planned just 
before the execution of the sprint based on the product 
backlog. Within the sprint the teams are self-organising and 
do daily Scrum, which includes daily stand-up meetings. When 
the sprint is over, it is reviewed and the Product Backlog is 
updated (Schwaber, 2017). Scrum framework accommodates 
a very adaptive way of working and no overall master plan is 
followed, but the project is pushed forward by the sprints.

ety of different methods and tools to conduct the project activities. In 
the following section the main and essential methods are described. 
The methods are categorised by the process phases. Furthermore 
icons indicate the intention behind the method. Throughout the pro-
ject we have selected the methods that we found most appropriate 
for the tasks at hand.

Literature Review
Description
A literature review is a way of ac-
cessing and analysing relevant liter-
ature on the topic being investigat-
ed. The purpose of a literature review 
depends on the overall research, 
whether it is qualitative or quanti-
tative or a mixed method approach. 
In the qualitative research the liter-
ature review is used for background 
research and framing the problem 
in the beginning of the study. (Cre-
swell, 2018) 
The literature review is conducted by 
identifying relevant keywords, ﬁnd-
ing articles and journals relating to 
these keywords and sorting the dif-
ferent journals and articles to make a 
literature map (Creswell, 2018)  
Our method approach
To gain insight in previous knowl-
edge, especially the ﬂuid balance 
charts and the practice around it, 
a literature review was conducted. 
Identifying keywords for the topic 
helped us to ﬁnd relevant material 
on our subject. The research base Pu-
bMed was especially useful. Group-
ing the different statement from the 
articles into categories helped ob-
tain a better picture of what relevant 
literature exists on the ﬂuid balance 
charts. 
Gathering 
Knowledge
Diverging
Internet-Mediated 
Research
Description
Internet-mediated research 
is a way to gather novel data 
for analysis that can provide 
new insight in relations to a 
research question (Fielding, 
2017).
Our method approach
Both in the beginning of the 
project and during the idea 
and concept development 
we have been using Inter-
net-mediated research. The 
prime goal of this method 
is for us to acquire sufﬁcient 
knowledge on a topic.
Gathering 
Knowledge
Diverging
Semi-structured 
interviews
Description
A semi-structured interview 
takes its starting point in an 
interview guide but allows 
the interviewer open-end-
ed exploration. With the 
semi-structured 
interview 
data can be gathered in a 
systematic way but still ac-
commodate exploration of 
new topics emerging dur-
ing the interview (Wilson, 
2014). 
Our method approach
Many of the interviews held 
during the project we chose 
to do semi-structured, be-
cause it enables us to di-
verge from the interview 
guide and react upon the 
situation. This way more di-
alog in the interviews were 
accommodated. 
At 
the 
same time, we had an inter-
view guide to ensure that 
relevant information would 
be gathered.

Observations and Contextual 
Interviews
Description
Taking ﬁeld notes on the behaviour 
and the activities that the users do 
is called qualitative observations. 
The ﬁeld notes are recorded in an 
unstructured or semi-structured 
way and are based on the activities 
at the research site. (Creswell, 
2018) Observants might take part 
in the activities depending on the 
situation. Asking supplementing 
questions based on the situation is 
called contextual interviews. This 
gives the interviewer a contextual 
starting point for the questioning. 
(Beyer, 1998)  
Our method approach
This method was used during the 
ﬁrst two visits at Herlev Hospital. 
We observed the nurses doing 
their work, morning meeting and 
patient contact. We asked clarify-
ing questions when needed. Ob-
serving what the nurses did and 
following up with questions about 
their workﬂows, tasks and respon-
sibilities, were very educational 
for us, due to the heavy amount 
of tacit knowledge hidden in their 
work practices.
Gathering 
Knowledge
Diverging
Tacit Knowledge Hotline
Description
A Tacit-Knowledge Hotline is a 
way to gather quick knowledge 
about something very speciﬁc. 
The hotline might be a person the 
project team knows and can be 
called whenever in the process it is 
needed. The person can help clar-
ify uncertainties as they emerge 
during the project, which can be 
small things about the workﬂow 
or the tacit knowledge only a per-
son on the inside would possess. 
This method has been developed 
by the project team.
Our method approach
During the project we needed in-
sights within different aspects of 
the nurses’ workﬂow. Using our 
network of nurses, that we could 
always casually contact, we could 
gain valuable insights on smaller 
questions like what happens on a 
ward round, or where and when 
do they use the rover. This knowl-
edge often had great importance 
on decisions, and made our pro-
cess more effective, since it ena-
bled us to make decisions quick-
ly based on the correct speciﬁc 
knowledge. The information gath-
ered this way is shown in work-
sheet 4. 
Gathering 
Knowledge
Development Arena
Description
A development arena is 
a cognitive space that 
entails 
a 
technological 
concepts, 
human 
and 
non-human actors, arti-
facts, social worlds and 
their interactions. The de-
velopment arena can be 
used to create a holistic 
overview of the concepts 
interplay with other are-
nas and their actors (Jør-
gensen, 2002).
Our method approach
We used the tool for map-
ping out the development 
arena in the beginning of 
the project. This method 
helped to understand the 
whole context and how 
the different stakehold-
ers in our project interact 
with each other.

Sequence Model
Description
The 
sequence 
model 
maps out how work un-
folds over time, which 
becomes a sequence of 
actions done by the user. 
The 
sequence 
model 
starts with a trigger, that 
kicks off a set of actions 
that accomplishes a task. 
(Beyer, 1998)
Our method approach
The sequence model was 
used as a part of the ex-
ploration of the context. 
Going through the steps 
of the nurses’ tasks very 
closely made sure we had 
a good understanding of 
their needs and their ca-
pabilities. 
Artifact Model
Description
The artifact model helps to 
understand the workﬂows 
around the artifacts. An arti-
fact can be everything from 
a to-do list, a form, a docu-
ment or a computer. The ar-
tifact model is a representa-
tion of the artifact mediated 
by a drawing, copy or similar, 
where callouts highlight dif-
ferent aspects of the artifact. 
(Beyer, 1998)
Our method approach
The artifact model was used 
to describe both the rover 
and the computer that the 
nurses use when they docu-
ment all their work. With this 
model we got to investigate 
the systems that the nurses 
have now.
Design Brief
Description
A design brief is a formulated prob-
lem statement from the client or 
company management. It reﬂects 
the starting point of a new project. 
The design brief consists of a set of 
goals, some constraints in which the 
goals must be met and criteria ex-
plaining how a successful solution is 
recognized. The design brief states 
the problem and the opportunities 
(Cross, 2008).
Our method approach
We used the design brief to concre-
tise our knowledge obtained during 
the exploration. By descripting the 
context, problem, goals and chal-
lenges we could specify what the 
project should solve speciﬁcally and 
what important goals to fulﬁl. This 
sparked a discussion on which areas 
of the problem were essential fac-
tors and what parts of the problem 
that we needed to leave out of the 
scope. The design brief also worked 
as a tool for conﬁrming that the 
team agreed on the scoping of the 
problem.

Brainstorm
Description
Brainstorming is a widely known 
creative method. The method is 
used for generating as many ide-
as as possible. The brainstorm-
ing takes its starting point with a 
problem that needs to be solved. 
The group then generates as 
many ideas as possible individu-
ally. Afterwards the group mem-
bers take turns reading one of 
their ideas. An important rule 
to brainstorming is that no crit-
icism is allowed, this could oth-
erwise kill the creativity. (Cross, 
2008)
Our method approach
We have used several versions 
of brainstorming e.g. Negative, 
brainwriting and brainstorming 
with different starting points. 
We have used brainstorming 
several times during our process, 
both for developing concrete 
ideas for the solution but also for 
more practical related tasks like 
ﬁnding a name for the solution 
or ways to collaborate with the 
end-users online. 
Persona
Description
Personas is a method where 
a couple of imaginary users 
are made up based on the 
different characteristics of 
the user segments. (Cross, 
2008) Creating a user proﬁle 
as close to the speciﬁc target 
user as possible. 
Our method approach
During the project four per-
sonas have been created, 
two nurses and two patients. 
Even though the personas 
model made us general-
ise both the type of patient 
and the type of nurse, which 
might not be representative 
for our end-users, the meth-
od still provided us with a 
clearer understanding of our 
users. 
User Scenario or Storyboard
Description
A scenario is a storyline of a us-
er-interaction. The storylines 
are constructed based on the 
user’s lifestyle and behaviour 
and tells how the user would 
use a product or a service 
(Cross, 2008). 
A storyboard is based on a vi-
sion for the use of a product 
or a service and outlines the 
steps chronologically. The tool 
was originally borrowed from 
the movie industry and the 
overall layout with pictograms 
and explanation are kept in 
the tool. (Beyer, 1998). 
Our method approach
Storyboards have been ap-
plied in most of our iterations. 
Making the ideas and solution 
into scenarios helped us to un-
derstand and evolve our ide-
as. The scenarios also worked 
great for mediating the ideas 
to the nurses and other stake-
holders.

SWOT
Description
SWOT is an analysis tool used 
for illustrating both internal and 
external conditions of a compa-
ny. The method puts Strength, 
Weaknesses, Opportunities and 
Threads in focus to draw a snap-
shot of the company compared 
to competitors (Ivækst, 2015)
Our method approach
The SWOT analysis was used for 
evaluating and developing our 
concepts. With this model more 
aspects of the concepts were 
unfolded, and the concepts were 
reviewed with a more critical per-
spective. The method helped us 
to look at the concepts in a more 
objective way and compare our 
concepts with each other. 
Afﬁnity Diagram
Description
The afﬁnity diagram gathers 
and sorts individual statements 
from different stakeholders to 
ﬁnd overarching patterns. It or-
ganises the different statements 
from stakeholders into common 
themes and issues. This way the 
most important issues are estab-
lished very clearly (Beyer, 1998).
Our method approach
The afﬁnity diagram was used 
for sorting all the inputs col-
lected from the survey. Using 
this method, it became clear to 
us, which statements the nurs-
es and the doctors had overall. 
Grouping the statements into 
categories helped to identify the 
biggest challenges for each of 
the concepts. Especially count-
ing how many of the test sub-
jects who said the same thing, 
gave an insight into how exten-
sive and crucial the different 
challenges were.

Weighted Objectives 
Methods (WOM)  
Description
The Weighted Objectives 
Method is a way to eval-
uate concepts and ideas 
against each other. The 
evaluation is based on a 
set of objectives, which 
are used to weigh the ide-
as against each other to 
identify what ideas fulﬁls 
the objectives best. This 
is done by giving scores 
on their performance for 
each of the objectives, 
which results in rank of 
the ideas. (Cross, 2008)
Our method approach
We have been using the 
Weighted objects meth-
ods several times to rank 
our ideas and concepts. 
Sometimes we have been 
scoring 
the 
concepts 
based on some prelim-
inary criteria and other 
times we have used it as 
an indicator for the us-
ers to rank and give their 
opinion on the concepts.

Prototyping
Description
Prototyping is used for me-
diating concepts to the rel-
evant actors, and to explore 
new areas of improvement. 
The prototypes enable the 
end-user to interact with 
the 
solution, 
providing 
them with a way to express 
concerns and insights (Bey-
er, 1998) 
Our method approach
Using the prototyping tool 
Axure made it possible for 
us to test the ﬁnal concept 
and afterwards reﬁne it. 
Prototyping in Axure ena-
bled us to show more than 
just wireframes of the solu-
tion, but rather a more in-
teractive 
and 
intelligent 
prototype (wireframe: very 
simple visualisation of a 
digital concept). The nurses 
had a better change of un-
derstanding the concept 
on a deeper level and could 
thereby provide more spe-
ciﬁc and relevant insights 
or concerns during the test. 
UX Principles
Description
UX design principles are a set of rules 
and heuristics that guides the graphic 
designer to produce user-friendly expe-
riences. There exist multiple design prin-
ciples but two of the most popular are 
the Gestalt Principles and the 8 Golden 
Design Rules:  
The Gestalt Principles are a set of rules 
that arose from phycology. The rules 
explain how the human mind tends to 
see objects by grouping related com-
ponents, 
recognising 
patterns 
and 
simplifying complex illustrations. The 
rules provide the designer with tricks to 
communicate the intended perception 
(Bradley, 2015)
The 8 Golden Design Rules are a list of 
principles that guides the UX designer 
with the focus of increasing the user’s 
productivity, by increasing feelings of 
competence and control (Shneiderman, 
2016).
Our method approach
During the prototyping of the apps, the 
graphic design was derived from these 
principles. For example, using a lot of 
white space to simplify, providing user 
control by adding feedback and options 
to cancel, grouping buttons together 
and separating information, amongst 
other aspects.

Flow Model
Description
The Flow model provides 
an overview of how the 
different actors interact 
and what their responsi-
bilities are. The interac-
tions are described with 
either tangible artifacts 
or intangible communi-
cation. The interactions 
are visualised by connect-
ing the actors by lines de-
scribing the direction of 
ﬂow and potential media-
tion artifact. (Beyer, 1998)
Our method approach
This method was applied 
to the different versions 
of the ﬁnal solution ad-
dressing how the actors 
would interact with each 
other using our solution.

Prototyping and Testing
Qualitative Usability Testing
Description
Qualitative usability testing is a 
UX based test that can help the 
designer identify problems in 
the concept, uncover new op-
portunities and learn about the 
end-users. During the test, the 
facilitator asks the test-subject 
to perform tasks, while observ-
ing the test-subjects behaviour, 
listening to feedback and asking 
follow up questions. The test can 
be done for multiple test-de-
signs and demands appr. ﬁve 
test-subjects for the results to 
be sufﬁcient (Nielsen Norman 
Group, 2020)
Our method approach
We performed a qualitative us-
ability test at Herlev Hospital to 
identify problems and oppor-
tunities within the, at the time, 
three different prototypes (red, 
blue and green), and conse-
quently get feedback to help de-
ﬁne the ﬁnal solution.

A survey studies a sample of 
a population to ﬁnd trends, 
and opinions or associa-
tions among variables with-
in that population group. 
The goal for the survey is to 
make conclusions about co-
herence within the sample 
population, which might be 
transferable to a broader au-
dience. (Creswell, 2018) 
Our method approach
Using a survey, we could 
gather substantial informa-

evaluation of our concepts. A 
broad audience of 122 nurs-

in the survey.

upon a business idea. It is 
meant as a communication 
tool to transmit a core mes-
sage of a business idea to 
potential stakeholders. The 
Business Pitch Deck can 
be adapted and presented, 
depending on the stake-
holders that needs to be 
convinced of the business 
idea. (Howard, 2019)

Pitch Deck as a tool to in-
vestigate speciﬁc areas of 
our ﬁnal solution and its 
market potential. The Busi-
ness Pitch Deck helped us 
clarify market size, custom-
ers, the business model, 
competition and ﬁnances. 
Providing us with an idea of 
which areas we needed to 
elaborate on.

The Combined Design-Approach Process
The actual work was initiated with the approaches and goals for the 
design process. The process ended up having ﬁve phases, starting 
out by scoping the project and deﬁning the problems, followed by 
idea generation and idea development, ending in concept develop-
ment and ﬁnally looking at the business perspectives. The process is 
illustrated in ﬁgure 22.
Each of the iterations had both diverging and converging activities 
as well as activities involving end-users (which is marked with green). 
Especially within the converging activities user input were crucial in 
order to make informed decisions and move forward towards one 
solution. Throughout the project our knowledge base of the context 
and the problems has been growing as illustrated in the ﬁgure. In the 
following each of the six iterations, the scoping and their activities will 
be elaborated on. 
Initial Research and Scoping
The project began with two visits to Herlev Hospital: One visit at the 
medical ward and one visit at the gastroenterology ward. Both visits 
lasted over four hours and gave a huge amount of insights to the con-
text, the nursing tasks and workﬂows. Because the ﬁeld visits were 
conducted before the project ofﬁcially started, our prior knowledge 
was extremely limited. 
Field Work
The ﬁrst visit was at the gastroenterology ward. The visit was in the 
morning to learn about the morning routines and observe a shift 
handover from night to morning shift. During the handover, informa-
tion about the patients and their treatment plans were passed on to 
the nurses taking over from the night shift. During the visit we fol-
lowed a nurse around the ward to observe her workﬂow and different 
tasks. This was done while spontaneously following up using contex-
tual interviews. See notes and pictures in worksheet 5. 
The medical ward was visited a week after the gastroenterology ward. 
This visit was a day-visit starting at 11.00 AM. Prior to the visit different 
questions for both patients, nurses and doctors had been formulat-
ed. These were based on our experience from the ﬁrst visit and ﬂuid 
balance registration in general. The questions provided a baseline for 
several interviews with nurses and one interview with a patient.  See 
questions, notes and pictures in worksheet 6.  
Observed Problems
After the visits at Herlev Hospital the different problem areas were 
outlined to help gain an overview of the problems around the ﬂuid 
balance charts, see ﬁgure 23. 
The issues surrounding the ﬂuid balance chart were plenty, which 
makes it complicated to point out a main reason for the ﬂuid balance 
charts to be an insufﬁcient solution.  
The initial research and scoping led to an 
understanding of the problem area. This 
section unfolds the ﬁeld visits to Herlev 
Hospital, the analysis methods for explor-
ing the context and the project scoping il-
lustrated in a Design Brief. 
Scoping

Analysing the Workﬂow
During the ﬁeld work, many new inputs and impressions were made, 
which resulted in a lot of unorganised inputs and knowledge. To sort 
the knowledge a sequence model was made. It provided an under-
standing and analysis of the nurses’ workﬂow, see worksheet 1. Break-
ing down the nursing tasks in steps revealed how many different 
tasks the nurses had just with regards to registering ﬂuid.
The nurses’ workﬂow includes the use of a number of artifacts. These 
were analysed in an artifact model to identify the work practices sur-
rounding them. The artifact model describes the roller computer, the 
rover and an overview of the SP interface. See the artifact model in 
worksheet 2 and 7.  
The FBCs are outdated and require handwritten data that needs to be 
additionally recorded in the SP, referred to as double documentation. 
The FBC is placed on the bedside table or the pin-up board in the pa-
tient’s room. The charts are ﬁlled out ideally every time a patient gets 
a new ﬂuid and every time the patient has consumed some. 
Scoping the Project with a Design Brief
A design brief was made to scope the problem. The following is an 
extraction of the design brief, see the full design brief in worksheet 8.
The design brief created a common understanding of the problem 
space and acted as a breeding ground for our exploration of the idea 
generation.

Figure 23 - Observed problems
Design Brief
Design context
Monitoring ﬂuid balance for the patients is a part of the nurs-
es' daily tasks. It is important to monitor due to the treatment 
course and keeping the patient well hydrated. With some dis-
eases and conditions, it is more vital to keep track of the ﬂuid 
balance, since it might otherwise have fatal consequences. 
The nurses can register ﬂuid at two levels: regular and extend-
ed, depending on the state of the patient. The ﬂuid balance is 
tracked using a ﬂuid balance chart - a simple piece of paper with 
a chart for recordings. (Fieldwork the 27th and 30th of January 
2020). The problem of accurately monitoring patient’s ﬂuid in-
take seems to be a worldwide problem.
Design problem
To gain the full overview of the ﬂuid balance the nurse needs to 
record seven different elements. If only one of the ﬂuid sources 
are registered incorrectly or not registered at all, the total cal-
culation will be incorrect. Nurses and doctors are very busy at 
all times. Lacking time might affect the FB tasks to get a lower 
prioritisation than other tasks. Furthermore, the data generated 
==PAGE:51==
Page 51 of 119
both on the FBC and in SP are not trustworthy due to the frequent errors (Field-
work the 27th and 30th of January 2020).
Especially recording the oral intake can be tricky. Patients or relatives might 
acquire drinks themself or they spill the provided ﬂuid. The ﬂuid balance chart 
is not very user friendly, and it can be difﬁcult to read when the information is 
transferred to the SP system. Studies show that only 25% of the charts are ﬁlled 
out correctly (Jeyapala, 2015) See ﬁgure below for overview over problems oc-
curring during both the normal ﬂuid balance and extended ﬂuid balance regis-
trations.
To gain a full overview of the ﬂuid balance creates 
multiple problems, yet the orally consumed ﬂuid 
seems to pose the most problems.  
The scope for this project is therefore to focus 
on improving the oral ﬂuid balance monitoring, 
while keeping in mind that the solution should 
be able to ﬁt with a solution that resolves the rest 
of the problems.
Design goal
 •
A more efﬁcient way of documenting OFB.
 •
Mitigated risks of malpractice
 •
A holistic solution that can be implemented 
anywhere it is needed
Design challenges
 •
Measuring the improvement of the solution.
 •
Fluid monitoring of accidental situations.
 •
Complex series of currently used products for 
drinking.
Extended ﬂuid balance
==PAGE:52==
Page 52 of 119
Iteration 1 - Idea Catalogue 
Brainstorm - Idea Generation
The idea generation began with a brainwriting session, where sev-
eral ideas were generated. This type of brainstorming was chosen as 
the initial, because we had created a lot of ideas in our mind that we 
needed to put in writing, to make room for more ideas to follow. See 
worksheet 9. After the brainstorm, the ideas were categorized into the 
following themes: ordering systems, weighing, devices like cups and 
straws, invasive methods, surveillance, nudging and wearables. 
Afterwards a negative brainstorming was conducted to broaden the 
solution space. This resulted in negative ideas like not registering at 
all or serving all ﬂuids for the day in the morning. Subsequently the 
bad ideas were turned into good ideas to reveal new solutions. See 
worksheet 10. 
To ensure a full exploration of the solution space a more restricted 
brainstorming was done. The solution was split into subfunctions: 
transfer to SP, registration, measuring, physical frame and container 
of liquid. Each of the subfunctions were unfolded with a brainstorm-
ing session. Combining different ideas from the different categories 
provided a bunch of new ideas. See worksheet 11. 
Not having the full understanding of either the problem or the con-
text enabled the brainstorming to scatter broad in the solution space. 
Our minds were not limited by legislation, feasibility of medical devic-
es or measuring devices. The ideas were gathered into 14 ideas repre-
sented in an Idea Catalogue. 
Exploration of Context
To get further insights and expand 
our knowledge about the context 
and the problem, an interview with 
Marie Lommer Bagger was ar-
ranged. Marie is a former nurse and 
has founded the company Measure-
let. Measurelet focuses on making 
it easier for the nurse to access the 
output FB data from the patients. 
She has a lot of knowledge on the 
context and the problems around 
ﬂuid balance. See worksheet 12. 
She gave advice both on how the 
project should proceed and which 
aspects of the context to focus on. 
Especially her knowledge on how 
beneﬁcial it can be to involve the 
patients in the registration was 
eye-opening, see ﬁgure 24. 
The Ideas in the catalogue are presented and described with strengths 
and weaknesses in worksheet 13. An overview of the idea headlines is 
seen in ﬁgure 25.

Catalogue
Iteration 1 describes the work from 
idea-generation, based on the design 
brief, to the idea catalogue and after-
wards to selecting ﬁve ideas.

possibilities. E.g. searches on both weighing scales and ﬂowmeters

to the hospital. The bracelets are equipped with a barcode that 
connects to the patient proﬁle in the EPJ system when scanned.

Iteration 2 - Five Ideas
Expanding the Solution Space 
Feedback on the idea catalogue sparked a discussion on how motiva-
tion plays a role in the solution, and how involved the patients should 
be. 
Motivating Nurses
Motivation for registering OFB is crucial for the solution to succeed 
in the end. Particularly because the lack of motivation for the current 
solution is a vital part of the problem. The ideas for solutions in the 
idea catalogue all focused on measuring ﬂuids precisely and register-
ing the ﬂuid consumption, not ensuring that the nurses will use the 
solutions. To ﬁgure out how to motivate the nurses research within 
the ﬁeld was conducted, see fact box: motivation. 
A way to motivate the nurses is to make them able to handle their job 
better. They would be able to provide better care, if they could easily 
document the patient’s ﬂuid balance. Therefore, the solution should 
provide the nurses with a more efﬁcient way of registering the ﬂuids 
to ensure that the data can be used appropriately. The solution ﬁrst 
and foremost needs to gather the needed data in an efﬁcient way be-
fore the data can be used for improving the patient care. Throughout 
the project this has been in focus. 
I2
Five Ideas
In Iteration 2 the ﬁve ideas were evolved 
with a storyboard to mediate the ideas 
at the theme-meeting at Herlev Hospital. 
Furthermore, motivation and patient in-
volvement were explored. Subsequently 
three ideas were chosen for further devel-
opment. 
Fact box: Motivation
There are a several theories made for motivation of staff in gen-
eral. As a leader you can e.g. reward employees, give respon-
sibility, put down realistic goals, give purpose, be transparent 
and use the employee’s strengths to motivate your employ-
ees (Pozin, 2020). Figure 29 shows a graph of main motivators.
 
Figure 29 - Motivation Factors
Money & beneﬁts are on the bottom of motivators (7%), in-
stead peer motivation (20%), encouragement (14%) and doing 
well (18%) tops the list. This indicates that a good working en-
vironment and team is more motivating than receiving high 
salaries or a bonus. (Square, 2020)
Motivating patients
There also exists some theories on how to motivate the pa-
tients to participate in and keep up their treatment (Wood, 
2020): 
 •
Create the goals for treatment together with the patient
 •
Set realistic smaller goals
 •
Explain why it is important to change behaviour
 •
Ask positive questions instead of negative: When did you 
have depression? changed to positive: What would life be 
like without depression?

Fact box: Patient Involvement
To accommodate the ﬁve megatrends the patients should play 
a more active role in their own treatment. In a questionnaire sur-
vey, the perception of user involvement is investigated among 
nurses and doctors. 
The survey shows that the perception varies a lot within the 
healthcare ﬁeld, but it suggests that most of the respondents 
understand patient involvement as: the patients are involved in 
the decision making and planning during the treatment course. 
This corresponds well with the legislation about getting a state-
ment of consent from the patients before starting a treatment. 
The survey also points out that the respondents think that pa-
tient involvement is about supporting the patients in self-car-
ing. Furthermore, the survey outlines barriers for the patient in-
volvement in practise, due to too unable patients, or the patients 
being unwilling to be involved (ViBIS, 2014). 
Another survey shows that most of nurses and doctors think 
patient involvement has many positive effects on the patient’s 
treatment course. The patients feel more comfortable and en-
gage more in their self-care when informed and educated about 
the treatment. This results in faster recovery and fewer errors. 
However, this survey has an unclear deﬁnition of the term pa-
tient involvement (Thusgård, 2018).
Patient Involvement
The interview with Marie Lommer Bagger sparked a curiosity for the 
ﬁeld of involving the patients. Looking into patient involvement, see 
fact box: Patient involvement, an important understanding of the 
term was realised: The nurses perceive the concept of involvement 
of the patients quite differently from our perception. We as design 
engineers think of user involvement as the patients taking an active 
part in the tasks concerning the medical treatment. Some nurses and 
doctors on the other hand, see patient involvement as patients being 
well informed about the treatment plan. However, for the ﬁve meg-
atrends to be accommodated, the patient involvement should be im-
plemented by the patients undertaking treatment tasks, while also 
being involved in the planning of the treatment. 
Mediating the Ideas with Storyboards 
The ﬁve ideas needed to be ready for presenting at Herlev Hospital as 
a part of a theme-meeting. 
To mediate our ideas, storyboards were chosen, since storyboards are 
good for describing the workﬂows around the ideas. The two perso-
nas Bent and Caroline were used as patients in the storyboards: 
The Tablet
The Tablet idea is based on having a tablet in each of the patient 
rooms. Here the patient or the nurse can type in the ﬂuid when it is 
being served and then again when it is consumed, see ﬁgure 30. This 
idea emerged from an iteration on the ‘Sticker System’ idea.  
The Dispensing System
The Dispensing System takes its starting point from a normal 
soda machine. Here the patient would have to login with his or her 
ID-bracelet to get access to the machine. It can then track how much 
the patient taps from the machine. With a smart way of measuring 
the leftovers, the system can identify the total amount of consumed 
ﬂuid, see ﬁgure 31.
Smart Cup 
A Smart Cup is an electronic cup that measures how much ﬂuid is 
consumed from it. The Smart Cup is connected to the patient by 
scanning the patients ID bracelet and uploads the data continuously 
to SP, see ﬁgure 32.

Scanning App
The Scanning App is a system, where all drinks come in pre-packed 
bottles or cartons. They all have a barcode that informs the system 
of the type and amount of ﬂuid. The patient and nurse have an app 
which can scan the barcode and easily register the drinks, see ﬁgure 
33.
The Necklace
The Necklace is a smart device placed around the neck that can 
measure how much ﬂuid is passing through the throat. The Necklace 
consists of an electronic part and a disposable ribbon. The Necklace 
needs to be linked to the patient’s ID. When the patient wears the 
Necklace, it can measure very precisely how much the patient con-
sumes, see ﬁgure 34. We were aware that this was likely technolog-
ically unfeasible, however this concept might expand their imagina-
tion of a potential solution.
