Qualitative and Quantitative Feedback 
Quantitative Feedback
The theme-meeting was hosted by the gastroenterology ward at 
Herlev Hospital who gathered all nurses and students doing differ-
ent projects in collaboration with the department. The meeting was 
a chance to exchange knowledge and feedback with 14 nurses from 
different backgrounds and specialisations. See worksheet 16.
We presented our project and then our ﬁve ideas to improve the ﬂu-
id balance monitoring. The nurses had the chance to ask questions 
and explain their concerns. Using a response sheet all comments and 
thoughts from the nurses were recorded. On the response sheet, the 
nurses could write thoughts for each of the ideas. Furthermore, they 
were asked to rank the different ideas by giving them a score from 
one to ﬁve (One being the lowest and ﬁve being the best). The rank-
ing gave insight on which of the ideas were favoured the most. 
The feedback resulted in the Smart Cup being chosen as the favourite, 
followed by the Tablet Concept. The Dispensing system and the Scan-
ning App got third place and The Necklace scored the lowest. Besides 
the ranking, each of the ideas got a lot of feedback, see worksheet 17. 
The main concerns for each of the ideas are described in ﬁgure 35: 
Overall, the theme-meeting provided us with valuable feedback. A 
suggestion was to combine the Scanning App with the Tablet Con-
cept. Furthermore, the solution should accommodate both able and 
unable patients. Having many different nurses evaluate the ideas 
gave valuable insights on how differently they understand the chal-
lenges and on how different their perception of how able the patients 
are to perform tasks. This might be caused by the nurses coming 
from different wards, doing different tasks and having different types 
of patients. 
Qualitative Feedback
To get more qualitative feedback on the ideas a meeting with a nurse 
from the obstetric ward (Pregnancy and Midwifery) was arranged. 
The obstetric nurse has been working for six years and has experience 
from both the obstetrical ward and the intensive care unit. She liked 
the Smart Cup the best, then the Scanning App and the Necklace.

Choosing Three Ideas
The feedback on the ﬁve concepts sparked internal discussion and 
made us re-iterate on the ideas, see worksheet 19. 
Upon the feedback from the theme-meeting, the obstetric nurse 
and the re-iteration, the Necklace and the Dispensing System were 
dropped. The Necklace posed too many feasibility and ethical issues. 
The Dispensing System was discarded, because the hygiene level 
would be hard to satisfy, and the leftovers would be hard to track. We 
chose to move forward with the Smart Cup, the Tablet and the Scan-
ning App, see ﬁgure 37.

into two different kinds of Smart Cups: One where electronics were 
integrated in a special cup (All-in-one) and one where the electronics 
could be attached to the normal glass and cups (Add-On), see ﬁgure

I3
Four Ideas
Iteration 3 explores the three ideas which 
turns into four ideas to cover new aspects. 
The four ideas were evaluated in several 
ways before moving on to creating con-
cepts. 
Further development of the ideas was conducted with brainstorms 
and discussions based on the concerns from the activities in the pre-
vious iteration.  For evolving the Smart Cups, exploration on existing 
technology was conducted, especially on the feasibility level, see fact 
box: Smart cups. 
Fact box: Smart Cups
The main insights gathered from this research 
was that some smart cups exist, however none of 
them can sustain being cleaned in a dishwash-
er, and especially not in the industrial scale dish-
washers. They must be hand washed. 
Example: Ember
Ember smart cup is a temperature regulating 
mug that is controlled through an app, see ﬁg-
ure 39. It can simply keep your coffee warm, and 
must be hand washed (Ember®, 2020). Price for 
one: 899,95 DKK.
The four ideas are presented with storyboards in worksheet 20. 
Figure 39 - 
The Ember 
Smart Cup
==PAGE:63==
Page 63 of 119
Evaluating the Four Ideas
The Corona Pandemic Hits
Initially the four ideas were meant to be tested at Herlev Hospital to 
see how the workﬂow would ﬁt. Testing the workﬂow and observing 
the actions could reveal tacit knowledge, which is hard to get else-
where. However, testing the concepts was unfortunately not possible 
due to the Corona pandemic. Herlev Hospital was closed for all un-
necessary activities. The corona-situation became an obstacle to our 
planned process, so we had to rethink our process to ensure the pro-
gress. A brainstorm on how to include the users digitally were made, 
see worksheet 21. Instead of testing our ideas with paper prototypes 
to see the workﬂow a test at a dormitory with engineering students 
was devised. Furthermore, four skype interviews with nurses were 
planned, and to get more quantitative feedback a survey was created. 
Survey with Storyboard Videos
The survey helped gather knowledge and feedback from several 
different stakeholders, both nurses, nursing students, doctors and 
medical students. The survey had ﬁve overall sections: introduction 
to the project and the survey, demography, the current FBC process 
(to make a baseline), presentation of the ideas and evaluation of the 
ideas, see worksheet 22 for the outline of the survey. The ideas were 
mediated with videos of storyboards, see storyboards in the pres-
entation of the concepts on page 67-70. The survey was distributed 
through research nurse Hanne Konradsen, personal network, and 
various Facebook groups for nurses and doctors. The survey got 122 
responses from various stakeholder groups, see worksheet 23. With 
the survey a baseline upon the current solution was made and insight 
to the current problem exposed, see worksheet 24.  
For analysing the feedback on the different ideas. All statements were 
coloured according to the profession of the respondent and then 
sorted into categories using an afﬁnity diagram, see worksheet 25. 
The main points for each idea are represented in ﬁgure 40. 
All the ideas got both good and negative feedback. Especially state-
ments on economy and technical feasibility were surprisingly well 
represented. 
Usability Test with Engineering Students
A usability test at a dormitory was arranged to investigate problems 
occurring during the workﬂow. The test was conducted with the use 
of simple paper prototyping artefacts to simulate the ideas. The test 
was conducted with six residents. Three different workﬂows were cho-
sen to be tested: The Tablet & Weight, one kind of Smart Cup and the 
Scanning app. The Tablet & Weight were made with paper and some 
simple wireframes. The Smart Cup was simulated with a normal cup. 
See test setup in worksheet 26. 
The test subjects were asked to ﬁrst simulate the current workﬂow 
using a FBC, identify what they thought was bad or good about it, 
and then they were asked to use the three new solutions. Hereafter 
they scored the ideas from 1-5 and wrote comments. This resulted in 
the Smart Cup getting most points, the Scanning App was second 
and the Tablet & Weight last. See results in worksheet 27. 
It was a bit difﬁcult for the test subjects to simulate the nurses’ work-
ﬂow, due to the missing tacit knowledge. Most of the test subjects 
were engineering students with no prerequisites for knowing any-
thing about how a nurse works. Because of the lack of knowledge on 
the workﬂow the test subjects could only do as we instructed them 
to. Which did not reveal new insight for us, but just conﬁrmed our 
expectations. 
Skype Interviews with Four Nurses 
The usability test did not give any new insights, so we needed to get 
qualitative feedback from real nurses. Luckily, it was possible to ar-
range four interviews with nurses over skype. To acquaint the nurses 
with the ideas, the survey was sent to them before the interviews. We 
assumed that it would be easier for the nurses to understand the ide-

as from the storyboard videos than us explaining it over skype. The 
interviews were performed using a semi-structured guide. 
Two of the nurses believed the Tablet & Weight was best and two be-
lieved that the Smart Cup was best. The Scanning App scored the 
lowest from all nurses, due to the uncertainty that leaving the respon-
sibility with the patient brings, see worksheet 28 for questions and 
feedback. 
After the different user evaluating activities, we still had trouble mak-
ing a ﬁnal decision for which ideas to move forward with. This was 
caused by multiple elements. Firstly, we did not have access to test 
with the nurses in their normal setting, furthermore we got a lot of 
diverging feedback. Some nurses liked the Smart Cup’s and others 
were more convinced of the Tablet & Weight. Some nurses wanted 
to engage the patients a lot, others did not like to give away the re-
sponsibility. If it had been possible to test the ideas workﬂows with 
nurses, a decision of which solution to move forward with, might have 
been easier. The workﬂow test at the dormitory only veriﬁed our own 
expectations to the ideas, the interviews with the nurses were contra-
dicting and ultimately all the ideas posed various issues according to 
the survey. 
So to approach the decision from another angle a weighted objects 
method model (WOM) was made, see ﬁgure 41. 
Figure 41 - WOM
The criteria in the WOM were based on the requirement speciﬁca-
tion, see worksheet 29, and main points from the design brief. This 
method indicates that the Smart Cups is the best solution. However, 
we had gotten very mixed feedback, we were not able to properly test 
the ideas and we believed that all of the solutions demanded more 
investigation for us to make an informed decision, see ﬁgure 42.
Figure 42 - Iteration on four ideas
Ultimately, instead of selecting some of the ideas to move forward 
with we continued with all four ideas to ensure that all of the potential 
was explored.

Iteration 4 - Four Concepts
Exploring Concerns
One reason that might have caused the doubt when selecting some 
ideas to move forward with, could be the several concerns that the 
ideas still posed. Due to the multiple aspects of the problem the solu-
tion needed to meet as many of these as possible.
Some of the concerns that all the ideas posed were: 
 •
How to integrate the other elements of ﬂuid balance registration 
like probe, medicine and intravenous ﬂuids in the solutions?
 •
How does the solution work for patients on restriction and on pre-
scription and how to accommodate both situations?
 •
How does the patient register multiple drinks? 
 •
How is it cleaned?
Furthermore, each of the ideas had speciﬁc uncertainties, see ﬁgure 
43. 
Figure 43 - Concerns and requirements for the four ideas
Based on the concerns and requirements for the four ideas, a brain-
storm was used to unfold the further potential of the ideas, see work-
sheet 30. From the broad range of activities and discussion made in 
Iteration 3, the four concepts were described more detailed using 
a SWOT analysis, describing the difference that matters in product 
drawing and scenarios, see concepts in ﬁgure 44, 45, 46 and 47. 
I4
Four 
Concepts
In Iteration 4 the four ideas were made 
into concepts and two was selected for 
further work.

Figure 47 - Concept 4, Scanning App

Page 71 of 119
Reaching a Decision
Visualisation Leads to Motivation
Some of the key aspects of the solution space was the motivation for 
the nurses to use the solution. A part of the motivation was assumed 
to lay within the continuous visualisation of the OFB which would en-
able the nurses to use the data they collected and adjust the care ac-
cordingly. Therefore, a brainstorm on how to motivate the nurses and 
how to visualise the data was made. See the brainstorm in worksheet 
31. The ideas generated were used as inspiration for how to integrate 
the motivation in the concepts.  
New Requirement Speciﬁcation
Based on the level of knowledge now obtained on the problem a re-
vised version of the requirement speciﬁcation was formulated, see 
worksheet 32. Especially usability for both the nurse and the patient 
were more detailed in this version.
Internal Discussion
In regard to move forward with the project and choose some of the 
ideas an internal discussion was conducted. 
During the further development of the ideas some of the concerns 
were rather difﬁcult to solve. This was especially for the Smart Cup, 
All-in-1, where the hygiene concerns and the feasibility of electronics 
in the dishwasher were especially hard to match. After a search on 
technical devices that could go in the dishwasher, the belief in the 
Smart Cup, All-in-one were close to none. The Smart Cup, All-in-1 were 
de-selected for further work. 
The Scanning App was primarily based on patient involvement, this 
posed too many responsibility issues and challenges about how the 
nurse should oversee the process. The Scanning App was also de-se-
lected for further work. By deselecting these two concepts the Smart 
Cup, Add-On and the Tablet & Weight were chosen for further devel-
opment. 
The arguments for choosing the Tablet & Weight, was to make a dig-
itized version of the current ﬂuid balance chart. This way the calcu-
lations, double documentations and misunderstandings around the 
handheld FBC could be avoided. This concept had many possibilities 
for incorporation of other registrations like medicine and food and ﬁt 
very well into the current workﬂow. 
The Smart Cup, Add-On, was more feasible than All-in-one. Having a 
device that can be attached to the cups and glasses, does not pose 
the same technical challenges and would still eliminate all the man-
ual registrations on ﬂuid intake. Cleaning of the solution would be 
possible with disinfection of the electronics and cleaning the service 
as normal. With the internal discussion of the concepts potential the 
ﬁeld was narrowed down to two concepts, see ﬁgure 48.
Figure 48 - Choosing two ideas

Iteration 5 - Two Concepts 
Further Development of the Two Concepts
The two concepts each had a set of requirements and some unan-
swered questions, see ﬁgure 49. 
To accommodate the requirements and unanswered questions the 
two concepts were further developed, see worksheet 33. 
Hygiene Rules
During the development of the two concepts the rules for hygiene 
were investigated. The investigation made sure that the ﬁnal solution 
met the legislation for the hygiene at the hospital. This could also re-
veal which of the concepts proposed new cleaning routines. 
To explore the hygiene legislations a meeting with a hygiene nurse 
was arranged. She evaluated the concepts from a hygiene perspec-
tive and broadened our knowledge, see worksheet 34. The hygiene 
nurse favoured the Smart Cup, but with the condition that it should 
be made with a smooth surface all the way around and with almost 
no ridges or holes for bacteria to grow. 
The concepts were further developed to meet the requirements, an-
swer the unanswered questions and too meet the legislation of the 
hygiene rules, see ﬁgure 50 and 51 for the two concepts. 
I5
Two 
Concepts
In Iteration 5, the two concepts are fur-
ther developed to accommodate some 
of the issues that were not solved yet. The 
two concepts are narrowed down to one 
ﬁnal concept.

Evaluation of the Two Concepts
To evaluate the two concepts against each other a list of the pros and 
cons for each concept were made, see ﬁgure 52. Furthermore, the 
essential assumption were listed as well. 
Figure 52 - Pros and cons
This revealed that the Tablet System had more directly time-consum-
ing tasks, like making the registrations, where the Smart Cup had 
more indirectly time-consuming tasks, like charging, disassembling 
and cleaning. The Smart Cup demands additional features around it 
to work: a place to store the devices, a charging unit, a cleaning proce-
dure and a quick overview of OFB data. Furthermore the Smart Cup 
exposed some paradoxes which might be difﬁcult to overcome, see 
ﬁgure 53. 
Figure 53 - Paradoxes on the Smart Cup
Besides adding the indirect workload, the Smart Cup also seemed like 
an over-engineered solution compared to the task of recording OFB. 
Furthermore, the development process of such technology would be 
quite extensive. The Tablet System on the other hand still requires 
manual registrations and eye-measurements.
To decide two scenarios were imagined:  
1. 
The Tablet System is chosen and functions as a digital FBC with 
the possibility of expanding features like registering food, protein 
shakes etc. If the Tablet System is implemented, the Smart Cup 
becomes irrelevant. This solution can be implemented quickly, 
and in the future most hospitals will have patient-used tablets an-
yway. This solution does not solve the problem as precisely as the 
Smart Cup since it still requires manual registrations.
2. The Smart Cup is chosen and functions as an autonomous regis-
tering once it is linked to the patient. The cups do not need to be 
replaced; however, they need to make room for a charging station 
and time for sanitising the electronics. The registrations are au-
tonomous, so the nurse gains a lot of time to do something else. 
However, this solution requires a lot of production work and could 
easily take 3-5 years to develop. The solution should still include an 
app that can provide overview.
0öƦǛƴŸöƴƴöēńöŭĚĚġƴöēńĻŸƞƴńġŭƼƞƦġ
RöƞĚƴŸĚġƴöēńĻŸƞƴńġƛöƴŊġŭƴ
0öƦǛƴŸƦƛŸƴĻŸƞƴńġŭƼƞƦġ͡ƴŸūöŞġ
ƦƼƞġƦńġƞġūġūĒġƞƦƴŸ
ĚŊƦöƦƦġūĒŢǛĒġĻŸƞġǕöƦńŊŭļ
RöƞĚƴŸƦƛŸƴĻŸƞƴńġƛöƴŊġŭƴ͡ƦŸƛöƴŊġŭƴ
ĚŸġƦŭŸƴĚġƴöēńŊƴöŭĚƦńŸƼŢĚƞġƦġūĒŢġ
ŭŸƞūöŢƴöĒŢġǕöƞġ
wöŞŊŭļƴńġńöƞĚǕöƞġǕŊƴńŸƼƴöŢŸƴŸĻ
ńŸŢġƦŸƞƞŊĚļġƦ
wöŞŊŭļöŭöƴƴöēńūġŭƴūġēńöŭŊƦū
==PAGE:76==
Page 76 of 119
Due to the fact that the Smart Cup needs an app for overview, that it 
will take a lot of development time, adds indirectly time-consuming 
tasks and adds new cleaning & charging procedures, it was deselect-
ed and the Tablet System was chosen for further development, see 
ﬁgure 54. The Smart Cup could be a great addition later on in connec-
tion with the possibility for upscaling the solution to include autono-
mous IV, medicine, feaces, urine and probe registrations. A complete-
ly autonomous FB monitoring. 
The Tablet System will provide the nurses with more time for other 
tasks, a possibility for patient involvement and a continuous overview 
of the OFB.
Figure 54 - Choosing one concept
Iteration 6 - One Concept 
Developing the Final Concept
After choosing the Tablet System as the ﬁnal concept, the develop-
ment of the interface began. To begin with, eight different interfaces 
were generated, see worksheet 35. From these interfaces an internal 
discussion was used to merge the interfaces into three that could be 
tested. The interfaces focused on differentiating overviews, differen-
tiating menus and differentiating ways of adding and ﬁnishing the 
drinks. The ﬁrst two interfaces were with mL measurements and the 
last one focused on a visual way of adding ﬂuids. 
The platform Axure was chosen as the developing platform for the 
prototypes. This platform enabled the prototypes to be more inter-
active and intelligent than normal wireframes and could calculate 
selected amounts of ﬂuids. The programme had a simple way of up-
dating variables that e.g. could sum the ﬂuids being served. Each of 
the prototypes made got a colour to better distinguish between the 
three solutions. 
The three prototypes are presented in wireframes in the next three 
pages.

Concepts
In Iteration 6 the ﬁnal concept is devel-
oped. This is done by looking at the inter-
face and testing different prototypes at 
Herlev Hospital. With the feedback from 
this test a ﬁnal solution called EquTjek 
was created.

Fact box:  Wireframes
A wireframe is a simple illus-
tration of the design of a web 
page or an app that demon-
strates which key functions 
and elements will be placed 
in the interface. The wire-
frame will typically indicate 
a simple user-interface ﬂow, 
and is hand drawn or quickly 
sketched using a wirefram-
ing tool, e.g. Adobe XD, Mar-
vel App, Axure etc. (Experi-
ence UX, 2020).

Figure 56 - The Blue prototype
The Blue
The idea behind the blue prototype is to use a ‘click & go’ principle. 
It should be fast and easy. So instead of having a slider or clicking 
on arrows, all relevant mL measurements are already presented (#3). 
When choosing how much ﬂuid is left (#5) the nurse must simply 
choose within preinstalled amounts. The menu in this prototype is 
always visible, to help make an easy overview, see ﬁgure 56.

Figure 57 - The Red prototype
The Red
The idea behind the red prototype is based on visual cues, meaning 
no mL measurements but rather a slider (#4) that anybody can use 
including patients, relatives and substitutes that have not received 
mL estimation training. choosing the container for the ﬂuid, sets the 
value of a full container according to the container size, see ﬁgure 57. 
==PAGE:80==
Page 80 of 119
Testing the Prototypes at Herlev Hospital
To evaluate the features in the prototypes and ﬁgure out which func-
tions would be best suited for further work a test at Herlev Hospital 
was organised. 
Setup
The aim of the test at Herlev Hospital was to identify which func-
tions in the prototypes the nurses liked and disliked. Furthermore, we 
wanted to see which prototype they liked best. Additionally, the test 
also aimed to ﬁnd out how accurate the nurses estimate the amount 
of ﬂuid that they serve for the patients. 
The test was conducted by turns with four nurses from different 
wards. The setup was in a room away from the ward due to corona. 
The nurses sat by a table with the prototype in front, a serving tray to 
the left and an imaginary patient table at the right as seen in ﬁgure 
58.
 
Figure 58 - Physical Test setup at Herlev Hospital
The nurses were asked to serve water and coffee for the patient and 
then register the amount on the prototype. They had to do this three 
times to try all three prototypes. While they registered the ﬂuids, the 
amount that they poured and registered on the prototype were re-
corded. This enabled us to compare the real amount with the regis-
tered. The nurses were asked to explain what they saw, thought and 
wanted to do while trying out the prototypes. See worksheet 36. 
Results
The nurses were generally very positive, liked the overall concept and 
could see the beneﬁts of an easy digital registration. They were in 
general quick to understand and use the prototypes. The nurses were 
asked which of the prototypes they favoured, this resulted in a scoring 
of the prototypes, ultimately the red one won, the blue came second 
and the green last. See ﬁgure 59. The individual feedback for the pro-
totype is shown in worksheet 37. 
Figure 59 - Feedback on the prototypes

For the overall concept the following feedback are seen in ﬁgure 60. 
Figure 60 - General feedback on overall concept
The test provided us with new insights on nursing practices. For in-
stance, that they calculate how much the patient has consumed and 
not how much is left in the container, see worksheet 37. Besides this 
the nurses really liked the feature of selecting the type of glass and 
cup, which could inform the system of the total amount of the con-
tainer. Combined with the slider function this would be a quite accu-
rate way of eye-estimating.
The test also held the element of testing the nurses’ capability to do 
eye-measurements. The served amount and the registered amount 
were in general more accurate using the red prototype compared to 
the other two. However, the cups we brought to the test were a bit 
bigger than the ones at the wards. This might have affected the nurs-

es’ estimations of the coffee. In all the estimations the nurses were 
only missing a maximum of 50 mL and on average about 20-30 mL. 
This is very little and has no major consequences for the patient. This 
reveals that the problem of precision might not be as big of a problem 
as we assumed it to be in the beginning. It also gives us the insight 
that nurses in general study the cup sizes before estimating amounts. 
EquTjek Was Formed
After the test, a ﬁnal solution with a rover app for the nurses and a 
tablet app for patients were chosen as a ﬁnal concept. The nursing 
app should take its starting point in the blue prototype but incorpo-
rating the help of the size of the different types of containers at the 
ward. This was chosen because it would have less steps than the red 
one. The tablet app should take its starting point in the red prototype, 
with selecting the container and then sliding according how load-
ed the container is. This way the Tablet System turned into the ﬁnal 
solution EquTjek, see ﬁgure 61. The name EquTjek is derived from the 
word equilibrium (Equ) a synonym for balance, and Check (red. Dan-
ish: Tjek) as in checking in with the patient. 
Figure 61 - EquTjek was formed

Highlights from This Chapter
 •
The process went through six iterations with diverging and 
converging activities based on our Combined Design-Ap-
proach Model.
 •
We went from: 14 ideas → 5 ideas → 3 ideas → 4 ideas → 4 con-
cepts → 2 concepts → 1 concepts, using several design meth-
ods for development and engaging the users. 
 •
It was a hard decision choosing between the Smart Cup 
and the Tablet System as the ﬁnal concept.
 •
The Tablet System became our ﬁnal solution; EquTjek.

FINAL 
SOLUTION
In this chapter the ﬁnal solution is presented and investigated us-
ing different analysis methods. The different versions of the solution 
are explained together with a representation of a ﬂow model around 
them. Finally, a business view is taken on the solution to explore its 
potential as a start-up.

ĻŸƞĚöƴö͌Rġŭēġ͇ƴńġI ĚöƴöǕŊŢŢĒġƴƞƼƦƴǕŸƞƴńǛ͌
Final Solution - EquTjek
After six interactions, one ﬁnal solution has been developed: EquTjek. 
EquTjek is a subscription-based service that is used to collect data 
on patient’s oral ﬂuid intake at hospitals. It can be used by both the 
nurses and the patients and makes it easier and more user-friendly to 
monitor the OFB. The solution consists of an app for the nurse on the 
rover, and a tablet-app for the patient. The backend database makes 
sure that data from the patient app can be accessed and edited from 
the nurse app, see ﬁgure 62. 
The Difference That Matters
EquTjek is based on the idea of an efﬁcient and user-friendly digital 
ﬂuid balance form. The concept will provide the nurses with quick, 
easy documentation and monitoring of the patient’s oral ﬂuid in-
take. This will result in mitigating or eliminating recognised problems 
around FBCs, explained in ﬁgure 63. 
Figure 63 - The Problem EquTjek Solves
The concept can furthermore reduce the workload for the nurses by 
adding a patient-controlled app on a tablet. This app makes the cog-
nitively able patients capable of registering their ﬂuid intake them-
selves. The app introduces a measurement function, without millili-
tres, using only visual cues, making it easy to use for the untrained 
eyes of the patient and relatives. Including the patient in the treat-
ment, is important to accommodate the ﬁve megatrends. The overall 
service from EquTjek are illustrated in ﬁgure 64:
Figure 64 - EquTjek Service
With less errors and time spent documenting, EquTjek can provide 
the hospitals with safer and better patient care.
User Activities
A storyboard has been made for both the nurse app and the patient 
app, to analyse the user activities and investigate areas of improve-
ment or overlooked difﬁculties, see ﬁgure 65 and 66. These story-
boards are also a great way of mediating the solution to potential us-
ers or other stakeholders.

The two storyboards are based on the personas; Bent and Caroline’s 
ability to register their own ﬂuid intake. Currently, it will always be the 
responsibility of the nurse to register the ﬂuid, but by placing the re-
sponsibility on the patient when appropriate, the nurse will free up 
around 15 min pr. patient to spend on other tasks, see worksheet 25.
It is still uncertain how the charging stations for the tablets will be 
placed or if there will be any, see ﬁgure 66, drawing #3. This will depend 
on the speciﬁc wards. Some might already have tablets installed in all 
patient rooms, other wards will only need a smaller number of tablets 
for the occasional FB patient. It will be possible to acquire EquTjek 
with full tablet implementation further described under ‘EquTjek ver-
sions’.  After all, the new Super Hospitals and the renovations of the 
old will include implementations of tablets in most patient rooms to 
accommodate the ﬁve megatrends. 
Prototypes
To illustrate the user-interface in both the patient and nurse app, two 
prototypes has been made in Axure. 
EquTjek For Nurses
Figure 67-A+B demonstrates the most essential interactions in the 
prototype. The interface is made for the rovers. It enables the nurses 
to register the patient’s ﬂuid intake in a more streamlined and efﬁ-
cient way. 
When the nurse needs to choose between patients it will simply be a 
list of the nurses’ patients pulled from SP, see ﬁgure 67 (#1). Hereafter, 
the nurse will access an overview of the patient’s progress, what is of-
fered on the bedside table and a possibility to register a new drink or 
jug (#2). The jug function will only be available in the nurse app, since 
they often serve a jug of e.g. water and can from the mL in the jug 
see how much ﬂuid is consumed. When the patient has drinks on the 
table, it is possible to register consumed ﬂuids in mL (#8 - #10). The 
consumed ﬂuid will then be visualised in the overview. If the overview 
is clicked, the nurse will access a history of the patient’s progress and 
information about their current status (#13). 
The overview is separated into the three daily nursing shifts from: 
06:00-14:00, 14:00-22:00, 22:00-06:00, see ﬁgure 68. This was request-
ed by the nurses to get an easier overview of how the previous shifts 
had handled the OFB. To get a quick overview of the balance, the 
nurse can see how far behind or ahead the actual OFB is compared 
to the calculated advised OFB. The overview in ﬁgure 68 on the left 
shows a dehydrated patient and the overview on the right shows a 
patient in balance. The advised OFB balance icon moves according 
to a calculation of the time versus OFB amount. The nurses’ tasks are 
then simply to keep up with the advised balance icon. 
Figure 68 - Nurse app OFB overview
The “click and go” principles were chosen for the rover app, due to ef-
ﬁciency. In the future there will be a library of different glasses or cups 
in the app, to help the nurses estimate the right amount, and the pos-
sibility to choose to estimate the amount using the slider function.

EquTjek For Patients
Figure 69-A+B demonstrates the most essential interactions in the 
prototype of the patient app. The interface is made for tablets. EquT-
jek for patients and relatives provides the users with a tool, where they 
can learn to manage their own ﬂuid balance. Being aware of your own 
ﬂuid balance as a patient, can have many beneﬁts.
The patient app has many of the same functions as the nurse app 
but works differently in speciﬁc areas. The patient will login using 
their ID bracelet (#1). When logged in, the patient will access their 
own OFB overview, progress and drinks offered on their bedside table 
(#2). However, if the patient is over hydrated or dehydrated the app 
will notify that it is unwise to drink more, or alert if they need to drink 
more (#3). When estimating mL amount, the patients will use a slider 
function, which is based on visual cues and not mL (#6). The nurses 
at the test at Herlev hospital agreed that the patients and relatives 
would beneﬁt a lot from this slider function, since they have a hard 
time eye-estimating millilitre. This method requires more clicks and 
time but is safer to use. The slider function is also used for updating 
how much ﬂuid has been consumed (#8).
The patient app overview is based on a more simplistic system. The 
advised OFB includes an icon that tells the patient if they are hydrated 
according to the time, which also moves by a calculation of time and 
ﬂuid balance amount. The patients overview also includes the offered 
drinks placed on the table. The overview on the left is an example of 
a dehydrated patient, and on the right a hydrated patient, see ﬁgure 
70.  Like in the nurse app, the patient’s task is simply to keep up with 
the advised icon.

To make the slider function work, it will be necessary in the future to 
make a library of most used glasses and cups at hospitals ﬁgure 69, 
(#5). The data gathered in the patient app, will always be available for 
the nurses in the nurse app and SP, so that they are able to supervise 
the progression or take over if needed.
SWOT Analysis
To look at the solution from a critical perspective, a SWOT analysis has 
been made, see ﬁgure 71. The analysis shows what potential threats 
could challenge our solution and what weaknesses it possesses, while 
also showing the strengths and opportunities that the solution has. 
The SWOT analysis shows that the strengths and opportunities out-
number the weaknesses and threats.
The biggest weakness for this solution is that there still is no guaran-
tee that the nurse or patient will actually remember to register the 
OFB. We assume that having a system that works well and is very us-
er-friendly is an instant motivator, however this is not veriﬁed. 
The solution has opportunities for expanding to other registrations 
like foods and medicine. The prototype includes a simulated menu 
where these registrations could be added, see ﬁgure 67 (#14) and 69 
(#11). There also lies an opportunity within upscaling the solution to 
serve home care and nursing homes, and in general expanding to 
other areas of the world, since FB monitoring is a problem in many 
other countries.

Page 96 of 119
It is possible to imagine that there, in the future, will be a threat of 
a non-invasive scanner that can calculate the patient’s current FB. 
However, at the time of writing, there are no consumable items that 
can measure FB by scanning the body. There exist some other FB 
monitoring apparatuses but none can measure oral ﬂuid intake that 
is not consumed through a probe. 
Locally, if the SP was to focus on implementing a user-friendly digital 
OFB registration, our solution could be threatened. However, there is 
no indication that SP would focus on this in the coming years since it 
ﬁrst needs to improve the essentials and work properly. 
EquTjek Versions
The next two sections examines the possibility for EquTjek to become 
a start-up, and unfolds some new aspects to the concept. EquTjek 
would be an external company providing OFB monitoring to hospi-
tals. EquTjek would have access to the data in SP and other EPJs. 
The nurses workﬂow depends highly on where they work, and they 
can have a lot of varying tasks depending upon ward. In some wards, 
the patients are very self-sufﬁcient, while in other wards, they are 
very insufﬁcient. Due to the varying demands from the wards, differ-
ent combinations of the solution were needed, to provide the best 
service for the hospitals. This resulted in the four versions of EquT-
jek described below; EquTjek Medic, EquTjek Full, EquTjek Premium 
and EquTjek Home, see ﬁgure 72. The versions are based on the idea 
that the user or customer can purchase a solution that is designed for 
their needs. Some wards might need a complete implementation of 
tablets, while other wards only need the nurse app. 
EquTjek Medic
EquTjek Medic is made for the wards that only need the nurse app. It 
will most likely be wards with patients that are on ﬂuid prescription 
and elderly, where the patients are less able to undertake responsibil-
ity for their FB. It will be purchasable for 15.000 DKK per month for 100 
licenses. The prices are calculated based on estimating how a viable 
business could be feasible.
To get a better understanding of the system that the EquTjek Medic 
package entails and how the different actors and artefacts interacts a 
ﬂow model has been made, see ﬁgure 73. 
In the EquTjek Medic package the nurse is the primary user of the 
app. The patient has no direct contact. SP or the local EPJ will provide 
data on patients and EquTjek then provides trustworthy OFB data, 
which is recorded by the nurse. The hospital will provide payment. 
The costs of hosting an app with GDPR data is not included in this 
network to simplify the illustration. 
Fact box:  FB Monitoring Apparatuses
Digistat Connect
The Digistat Connect collects data on alarms and events from 
medical devices e.g. infusion pumps, ventilators and patient mon-
itors and provides an automatic patient data ﬂow (Ascom.com, 
2020). 
Volumetric Pumps
Volumetric pumps are a type of infusion pump that can deliver a 
steady ﬂow and a speciﬁc amount of ﬂuids, very fast or very slowly 
(CADTH, 2020)

EquTjek Full
The EquTjek Full solution includes both the nurse and patient app. 
This package is for the wards that have patients that can undertake 
their OFB registrations. In the future, this will likely become the pre-
ferred solution, as the patients will get steadily more involved in their 
treatment. They will be able to purchase 100 licenses for 100 nurses 
and 100 patients for 20.000 DKK per month. The patient app will not 
be available to purchase by itself. There will always be a need for the 
nurse app, so the nurse can take over if needed e.g. if the patient is 
elsewhere or asleep, see ﬁgure 74.
In the EquTjek Full package both the nurses and the patients are the 
primary users. The patient receives a tablet with the app installed, 
and they then provide trustworthy OFB data back. The patient app 
also includes educational videos and guides on how to record OFB. 
This is especially useful since many patients need to continue OFB 
monitoring when released from the hospital. The patient app on the 
tablet also serves as a tool for easy OFB overview during the daily 
ward rounds. The nurse can always access updated OFB data on the 
patients from the nurse app and SP to supervise. 
EquTjek Premium
The EquTjek Premium package is meant for the wards and hospitals 
that need both the apps but also installation of tablets. This could for 
example be wards in Herlev Hospital, and in general many of the older 
hospitals in Denmark. The package comes with a down payment for 
the instalment adjusted according to the amount of tablets needed, 
and a monthly payment of 20.000 DKK for 100 patient and 100 nurse 
licenses, see ﬁgure 75. 
EquTjek will pay for the tablets and afterwards install them in the hos-
pital. The implementation and installation process for this is, for the 
time being, black boxed, see fact box: Black boxing. The interaction 
between the stakeholders is otherwise the same as the EquTjek Full 
package. 
EquTjek Home
EquTjek Home is meant for the former patients, who will need to con-
tinue OFB monitoring when released from the hospital. The patients 
have learned to use the system in the hospital and can then buy a 
monthly subscription for 50 DKK, to continue using the system that 
they already know. 
EquTjek Home will cooperate with the local EPJ, to maximise the use 
of the OFB data and for the EquTjek app to receive data on the pa-
tients. The private users, most likely former patients, will provide pay-
ment.
Fact box:  Black boxing
Black boxing describes a system which converts inputs into the 
desired outputs without describing the function happening be-
hind the conversion. The black box functions is unexplored but 
the effect is known (Cross, 2008), see ﬁgure 76.
Figure 76 - Black boxing

Market & Business Analysis
A market and business analysis has been made to investigate the start-
up potential for the concept. The analysis is based on the Business 
Pitch Deck method, used mainly for pitching start-ups to investors. It 
provides a broad and general overview of the business potential.  
Customer
The users of the EquTjek platform are the nurses and patients, but the 
primary customer will be the individual hospitals or regions. Herlev 
Hospital has initiated this project based on their need for better ﬂuid 
balance monitoring and are highly interested in a solution. The busi-
ness would be B2G (Business to Government). 
Market
The ﬁrst market to target will be the Capital Region of Copenhagen 
and Region Zealand, both regions use SP as their EPJ. These two re-
gions have 16 hospitals which translates to a market size worth 1,9 mil-
lion DKK annually (calculated from 20.000 DKK monthly payment per 
hospital). Hereafter, the rest of the regions in Denmark would be tar-
geted. Denmark has 27 hospitals which equals 3,2 million DKK mar-

ket size. Monitoring the ﬂuid balance is a worldwide problem, so to 
begin with, an upscaling to the Nordic countries, Great Britain and 
Germany will be targeted. The whole of the EU has 21.000 hospitals = 
2,5 Billion DKK market size annually, see ﬁgure 78. 
Figure 78 - Market size
The need is continuously increasing due to the growing elderly popu-
lation who are often on ﬂuid balance monitoring. The healthcare mar-
ket is a stable market that will likely only grow with the population 
size.
Competition
There are no direct competitors other than a small Danish start-up 
that works with ﬂuid balance but on the aspects of faeces and urine 
ﬂuid output. Herlev Hospital initiated the project based on a need for 
a solution, which indicates that there are no alternatives. Figure 79 
shows our competitive advantage over the current solution.
Figure 79 - Competition
Business Model
The solution consists of 4 versions; EquTjek Medic, EquTjek Full, EquT-
jek Premium and EquTjek Home see ﬁgure 80. The business model 
for EquTjek is based on a subscription revenue model. We will receive 
monthly payment from the primary customer: The hospitals, but also 
from private users. If the customer buys the EquTjek Premium, then 
the ﬂow to the hardware supplier is relevant.
Implementation Plan
An implementation plan has been made for the ﬁrst 1,5 years, see ﬁg-
ure 81. The ﬁrst course of action is to conduct a test at Herlev Hospital. 
This is scheduled to start in September, while we are expecting to 
receive a letter of interest from Herlev Hospital as well. We will simul-
taneously seek funding of 200.000 DKK, while striving to attend the 
Inno-Founder programme in January 2021. 
The app will be used within healthcare and will therefore need to be 
ﬁnished and CE labelled before we can release it to the Danish mar-
ket. This is estimated to be done at the end of 2021. We expect to 
outsource the early app development to a student software course at 
DTU.

Finances
With an investment of around 200.000 DKK, it is feasible for us to turn 
EquTjek into a viable business, see ﬁgure 82, given that we can get 
help from Skylab, and other initiatives for start-ups at DTU. We will 
break-even in the middle of 2021. The calculation of the monthly pay-
ment is based on assumptions about a feasible business, the amount 
is not determined yet. 
Figure 82 - Finances
Risks and Needs
To ﬁnance the start-up EquTjek needs an investment of appr. 200.000 
DKK. This enables the start-up to ﬁnance the test at Herlev Hospital 
and other early legislation and patent investigations, development of 
the app etc. See ﬁgure 83. 
The biggest risk is the possibility that the hospitals are being hard to 
convince, due to the hospitals having a limited budget. However, we 
believe that having Herlev Hospital as a key player in our project we 
can overcome this challenge. There is also a risk of SP and other EPJs 
not being willing to cooperate for unforeseen reasons. 
Figure 83 - Risks and needs
The business analysis illustrates that the start-up EquTjek has a poten-
tial of being a viable and feasible business. An investment of 200.000 
DKK is not unrealistic, and the development costs are quite low, espe-
cially if our team can undertake some of the app development. Even 
more if the risks are investigated and possibly mitigated. There would 
be a need for competencies within business and sales strategy and 
within GDPR and general legal matters.

Discussion of EquTjek
The solution has been developed and analysed from different angles, 
and consequently, some uncertainties within the concept have been 
discovered. 
First, the concept has not been tested in a real setting. We have done 
some usability testing to simulate a real scenario, however the goal 
for the ﬁnal tests at Herlev Hospital was to test the user-interface and 
not particularly the user activities or user workﬂow. This means that 
we have not tested the ﬁnal concept. Had we had the possibility for 
testing with real patients and in a real workﬂow, we could have evalu-
ated the ﬁnal concept more thoroughly. This was of course not possi-
ble due to the Corona pandemic. 
Another aspect to include in the future test is the accuracy and reli-
ability of the patients’ recordings of their own ﬂuid balance. Will they 
skip registrations just to look good on paper? Or will they be more 
precise than the nurses? This is unknown, but we have assumed that 
patients will be honest since it is their own health at risk, and we 
have understood from nurses during interviews that they do not be-
lieve that cognitively able patients will in general “cheat” (Fieldwork, 
30th of January 2020). Furthermore, we have assumed that given the 
amount of downtime that the patients have, they will perform the 
registrations much more precisely than the nurses.
The test should also include a result with regards to the nurses’ will-
ingness to use the app. We cannot  say for certain, that the nurses 
will prefer the app rather than the current chart, however we have 
only received extremely positive feedback from all nurses and doctors 
we spoke to during this project when suggesting a more streamlined 
process for OFB documentation. Furthermore Herlev Hospital has in-
itiated this project to solve the problem, which indicates that a need 
exists. 
Through the last iterations we were going back and forth about choos-
ing the right concept for the ﬁnal solution. It was a close run between 
the Smart Cup and the Tablet System. It came down to choosing be-
tween quick implementation or the more efﬁcient solution. Ultimate-
ly we chose the Tablet System due to the indirect impracticalities of 
the Smart Cup. However, we did not have any possibilities for testing 
this with users in a real workﬂow. Would the Smart Cup have won if 
it could have been tested? The Smart Cup is certainly more direct-
ly timesaving, so would a test have resulted in deciding that it was 
worth the indirect impracticalities? Or would the test have resulted 
in the Tablet System winning due to the patient involvement and the 
similarity and familiarity to the current process? 
The nurses were also torn between the two concepts: 50% chose the 
Tablet System and 50% chose the Smart Cup during the skype inter-
views, see worksheet 28. 
EquTjek has potential but also concerns and uncertainties, which 
needs to be tested and investigated before it can be developed fur-
ther. An extended version of the prototypes will be the boundary ob-
ject when testing EquTjek in a real workﬂow at Herlev Hospital.
==PAGE:107==
Page 107 of 119
Highlights from This Chapter
 •
EquTjek solves the FBC problems by providing efﬁcient & 
user-friendly documentation, patient involvement, less 
likelihood of malpractice and trustworthy data.
 •
EquTjek has more strengths and opportunities than weak-
nesses and threats.
 •
EquTjek consists of four versions: EquTjek Medic, EquTjek 
Full, EquTjek Premium and EquTjek Home.
 •
EquTjek has a potential for being a viable business.
 •
EquTjek has not yet been properly tested due to the Corona 
pandemic.

This chapter will summarize the project. It provides a discussion on 
our design process, decisions made and an evaluation of the collab-
oration.  Following a summary of our learning output, recommenda-
tions and future work.

Outro
==PAGE:110==
Page 110 of 119
Design Process Discussion
Our process has in many perspectives lived up to our intentions and 
expectations, but there is still room for improvement and reﬂections.
  
Building the design process on an iterative approach with many at-
tempts to test the ideas and concepts enables them to be modiﬁed 
several times. However, looking back at the process, a more thorough 
initial research on the context and the complex problem could have 
beneﬁted the idea generation in the beginning of the project to bet-
ter ﬁt the system. We went on ﬁeld work having no prior knowledge 
about the workﬂow in a hospital. This meant that we did not have 
a primed mindset, but it also meant that we spend some time un-
derstanding aspects that might have been better understood if re-
searched beforehand.
Throughout the process there have occasionally been times where 
we have been in doubt of which activities to conduct in order to move 
forward. Instead of discussing the possibilities thoroughly we carried 
out several activities and methods to see which would be most bene-
ﬁcial. As an example: In iteration 3 we had trouble deciding, so we tried 
out several things to converge; WOM, usability test with engineering 
student, semi-structured skype interview and a survey. In some cases, 
an internal discussion upon which activities to do might have provid-
ed us with a more targeted approach and saved us some time. On the 
other hand, we could learn a lot from each attempt about why the ap-
proach did or did not work and move on to the next activity if needed. 
Another aspect that has affected our process is our prior experience 
in design projects. From the completion of several previous projects 
during our studies, we have tried and evaluated countless methods, 
tools and approaches. The experience has made us very conﬁdent in 
what tools and frameworks to apply, what we believe works well and 
what to avoid, which might have had a downside. Investigating and 
exploring new methods and tools we could have used, has been min-
imal. E.g. when mediating the ideas and concepts to the users, the 
storyboards have been in use several times, we did not investigate 
whether another method might have been better for the purpose. 
But on the other hand, we know that this method works. 
We have also experienced from this project that healthcare research-
ers and engineers work and think differently. It is clear from the lit-
erature on the problems surrounding the FBCs, that healthcare re-
searchers often tend to blame the staffs procedure for causing the 
problem e.g. if there are mistakes happening, then the solution must 
be to improve upon the healthcare personnel, by increasing aware-
ness and education. Engineers on the other hand, when investigat-
ing a problem, tend to blame the product e.g. if there are mistakes 
happening, then the solution must be to improve upon the product. 
It seems that the two mindsets can beneﬁt greatly from each other. 
Compared to previous projects the collaboration with the end-users 
has been considerably easier in this project. This is mostly due to the 
project setup with Herlev Hospital and Hanne Konradsen. Having 
a contact on the inside that knows the infrastructure of the organ-
isation, the stakeholders and can mediate the contact is extremely 
beneﬁcial when collaborating with an external company. Hanne has 
been able to advocate the project and to create interest from several 
nurses. Having Hanne set up meetings, interviews and tests for us has 
saved us much time and energy. Even before we started the project, 
Hanne arranged ﬁeld visits to Herlev Hospital, this kick-started the 
project much faster than usual. 
When exploring the complexity of the healthcare system we realized 
how much tacit knowledge there exists within the ﬁeld of nursing, 
and how various aspects of procedures and circumstances affects 
their workﬂows. Having access to ﬁrst and foremost Herlev Hospital 
but also having a tacit knowledge hotline during the project has been 
very beneﬁcial. This was especially true when knowledge about spe-
ciﬁc procedures was needed to make decisions for the development. 
==PAGE:111==
Page 111 of 119
Having someone to contact and getting quick responses enabled the 
process to run smoothly and enabled us to accommodate our lack 
of tacit knowledge in the ﬁeld. Our prior knowledge was very limited 
and might have enabled us to think outside the box, more than we 
otherwise had been capable of. 
The tacit knowledge in a complex “closed” system like healthcare is 
very high. For example; when designing a new kitchen machine, most 
likely you and the team behind has tacit knowledge within the ﬁeld, 
since we all have some sort of experience. In a “closed” system like 
the healthcare system, the tacit knowledge is only achievable to the 
people working within the system. This is part of the reason why the 
usability test with the engineering students did not provide any new 
insights, if we had tested a new form of food mixer the test-subjects 
would likely have had some prior experience to pull from, and the test 
would have worked.
One aspect where we did not involve the users was the brainstorm-
ing and idea generations. This could have been staged as a workshop 
making the nurses brainstorm on ways to solve the issue. This could 
have generated some interesting ideas from the systems experts. We 
chose not to involve the end-user in this way due to their working 
environment, where they do not have time to attend to much else 
during their shifts. A possibility was then to host a workshop in their 
free time, but this quickly became impossible and irresponsible due 
to the Corona pandemic. However, the nurses engaged a lot during 
the different feedback sessions and suggested many ideas of com-
bining ideas or adding additional features. 
When the Corona pandemic hit Denmark, our process was set on 
hold. Our planned process of testing the ideas with paper prototypes 
and ﬁguring out how the actual workﬂow with the new ideas would 
look like, were suddenly not possible. Instead we had to rethink how 
to engage the users in a digital way. This was interesting in terms of 
how to collaborate without being present physically and it broadened 
our horizons. We managed to get feedback through skype interviews 
and a survey. The extensive feedback from the survey was certainly 
plentiful and mostly helpful, but not being able to completely control 
the direction of the feedback, meant that we received comments on 
e.g. misunderstandings in the storyboard and the technological and 
economical feasibility of the concepts. These are unrelated to the pur-
pose of the survey, and comments that take up the mind of the re-
spondent, who then does not comment on the idea. Doing in-person 
interviews can certainly not get as quantiﬁed, however the facilitator 
can steer the feedback in the right direction. 
One aspect that we have been in doubt about throughout the project, 
was whether the patients could be considered as a part of the users 
of the solution. One reason for this were the contradicting opinions 
from the nurses about how much the patients are capable of. The fact 
that the patients on the different wards are extremely diverse, makes 
the nurses opinion on this contradictory since they all have very differ-
ent experiences. If we had been more consistent on whether patients 
should be included or not, it could have helped us make decisions 
along the way.  
We have designed a solution that we believe will solve many of the is-
sues surrounding the FBC. We are aware that a better solution would 
be a non-invasive way to instantly measure FB in the body. But before 
this is invented, the EquTjek solution provides a tool for easy docu-
mentation that can help the nurses provide better care and is one 
step closer to a problem-free FB registration. The solution however 
is not fully developed yet, it will transform and evolve during further 
testing and development.
==PAGE:112==
Page 112 of 119
Our Learnings
We have through this master thesis proven that we can:
Use different design approaches like the stage-gate mod-
el and agile thinking to manage and organize a bigger de-
velopment project, that goes from a need to a solution in a 
complex system. We have developed a new model; the Com-
bined Design Approach that we believe is more suitable for 
solving problems within a complex system like the health-
care sector.
Use design methods and theories to develop a solution. The 
process has been repeatedly diverging and converging. The 
methods have been chosen appropriately to ﬁt the teams 
needs to be able to proceed or make decisions.
Successfully explore and scope the problem around a com-
plex system like healthcare in general and ﬂuid balance. 
Through exploration of context and exploration of technolo-
gy we have investigated the problem area before scoping it 
appropriately in a design brief, targeting a tangible problem 
in a complex context.
Gather knowledge on design problems and challenges. We 
have through the whole process kept exploring the complex 
system to be able to make informed decisions that will result 
in an information-based solution, which in the end will ben-
eﬁt the users.
Stage ﬁeldwork resulting in relevant and applicable data. 
The ﬁeldwork has been scheduled to ﬁt with the teams need 
to receive feedback or to explore context. The team has suc-
cessfully mediated the concepts through storyboards to the 
users throughout the project to be able to get relevant and 
informed feedback.
Engage with relevant stakeholders using appropriate design 
methods. The engagement of the users especially the nurs-
es have been planned and conducted in several stages of the 
process with appropriate methods. Especially in the form of 
feedback on the ideas and concepts.
To quickly acquire new prototyping skills in Axure necessary 
for prototyping digital solutions, which could be used during 
usability tests with users.
Use theories of business and entrepreneurship to explore 
the possibilities of a feasible business. We have used the 
business pitch deck as a tool to analyse the possibilities of a 
feasible start-up based on EquTjek.

Recommendations
A list of recommendations has been conceived based on our experi-
ence from this project within healthcare. The recommendations are 
relevant for stakeholders involved in similar projects, developers; de-
sign engineers and healthcare professionals.
1. Share the Needs and Problems 
The unfulﬁlled needs and problems that occur within a system, 
like the healthcare system, is very hard to know for external stake-
holders. Engineers often do not have any tacit knowledge that 
leads to the insights required to discover the needs and problems. 
It is therefore very beneﬁcial for the holistic system, the workers 
within it and the engineers to communicate or advertise these 
areas of improvement. Reach out and share the needs.
2. An Internal Contact in the Organisation
It is highly beneﬁcial for engineers to have a contact within the 
system that can set up interviews with users and other actors. 
Getting in contact with relevant users is extremely challenging 
and can demand a lot of time for the engineers. The more users 
that are interviewed the better the ﬁnal solution will be. 
3. Access to Tacit Knowledge
It is also highly beneﬁcial for engineers to have a contact with tac-
it knowledge insights. Throughout the project, we have been lack-
ing knowledge about things that may seem very small but has 
had a huge consequence for the decision making. To gain these 
insights we have had a casual contact within the system that could 
answer these questions quickly. We have named this method of 
exploration; Tacit Knowledge Hotline. Having an informed base to 
make decisions on will beneﬁt the solution in the end.
4. Different Viewpoints Beneﬁt from Each Other
Healthcare researchers and engineers think and work different-
ly. Research within healthcare often focuses on the problem’s or-
igins, what the problem is, how big, and why it is important, not 
particularly how it can be solved. Engineers on the other hand 
tend to focus on the opposite, solving the problem the best way 
possible. Therefore, the two forms of research can beneﬁt greatly 
from each other. 
5. The Best Solution is not Always the Obvious
Design engineers are experts in exploring a, to them, unknown 
system. When collaborating with design engineers you are en-
gaging with an engineer that will try to solve the problem in the 
best way possible. When reaching out, the initiator should not ask 
for the development of a speciﬁc idea for a solution but asks for a 
problem to be solved. Design engineers could produce what has 
been requested, but very often the best solution is not the most 
obvious, and the problem might not be as ﬁrst anticipated.
==PAGE:114==
Page 114 of 119
Future Work
EquTjek entails some concerns and uncertainties that needs to be in-
vestigated in the future work. This section ranks what concerns that 
are most critical, in terms of product development and start-up per-
spectives, and what activities should be done to investigate and mit-
igate them. 
1. Does the Solution Work? 
The ﬁrst critical step is to test the solution. Many concerns lie 
within the actual use of EquTjek that will shape the direction of 
the further development. The test should answer these questions:
 •
Are the apps creating the desired effect and what functions 
can be improved upon?
 •
Will the nurses be more motivated to register OFB?
 •
Are the patients’ registrations accurate and reliable?
 •
Is it safe to let the patient register their own OFB?
Activity: To perform the test, it will be necessary to make a new 
prototype that can manage a backlog of data and calculate reliable 
data. The test might start around September at Herlev Hospital. 
2. Will the Hospitals Purchase the Solution?
There will simultaneously be a need for investigation of the cus-
tomers willingness to purchase the solution. There are two likely 
customers; either the solution is purchased by the individual hos-
pitals or by a whole region. 
Activity: A meeting with the procurement department at Herlev 
Hospital would be extremely beneﬁcial, both regarding calculat-
ing a feasible price and to explore the possibilities for selling and 
upscaling.
3. How to keep the Project going?
To keep the project going, it will be necessary to receive funding. 
Activity: There are many possibilities at DTU and through different 
start-up programmes to receive funding. The team could for ex-
ample apply for acceptance into the Innofounder Programme in 
January. 
==PAGE:115==
Page 115 of 119
The un-intuitive and un-efﬁcient FBC is the central problem area re-
garding oral ﬂuid balance documentation. To resolve this, we have 
developed a new digitised documentation system; EquTjek and have 
thereby eliminated many of these problems. EquTjek introduces a 
more streamlined documentation process through an app that is 
integrated in the nurses existing rovers and the nurses’ workﬂow in 
general. This results in, efﬁcient & user-friendly documentation, less 
likelihood of malpractice and trustworthy data. 
Furthermore, the responsibility for the registration is moved onto the 
patient when appropriate. This gives the nurses more time to spend 
on other tasks, while the ﬁve megatrends in healthcare are accom-
modated by introducing digitised patient involvement. A small step 
to secure a future with a healthcare system that can handle the in-
creased demands. 
There are still some uncertainties within EquTjek which needs to be 
tested and investigated. This will be the ﬁrst course of action for fu-
ture work. 
Through the project we have navigated a complex system, success-
fully explored needs and solutions, included relevant stakeholders 
and used relevant design theory in practise. We have from experience 
in this project formulated recommendations for innovation projects 
within healthcare, to mediate our ﬁndings and contribute to better 
innovation in the healthcare system. 
CONCLUSION
